id="content" class="mw-body" role="main">\n\t\t\t<a id="top"></a>\n\t\t\t<div id="siteNotice" class="mw-body-content"><!-- CentralNotice --></div><div class="mw-indicators mw-body-content">\n<div id="mw-indicator-featured-star" class="mw-indicator"><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Featured_articles" title="This is a featured article. Click here for more information."><img alt="This is a featured article. Click here for more information." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/e/e7/Cscr-featured.svg/20px-Cscr-featured.svg.png" width="20" height="19" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/e/e7/Cscr-featured.svg/30px-Cscr-featured.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/e/e7/Cscr-featured.svg/40px-Cscr-featured.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="462" data-file-height="438" /></a></div>\n</div>\n<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Benjamin Britten</h1>\t\t\t<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">\n\t\t\t\t<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>\t\t\t\t<div id="contentSub"></div>\n\t\t\t\t<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>\t\t\t\t<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>\n\t\t\t\t<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>\n\t\t\t\t<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">"Britten" redirects here. For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Britten_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Britten (disambiguation)">Britten (disambiguation)</a>.</div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">"Ben Britten" redirects here. For the British scientist, see <a href="/wiki/Ben_Britton" title="Ben Britton">Ben Britton</a>.</div>\n<p class="mw-empty-elt">\n\n\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Benjamin_Britten,_London_Records_1968_publicity_photo_for_Wikipedia.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Benjamin_Britten%2C_London_Records_1968_publicity_photo_for_Wikipedia.jpg/220px-Benjamin_Britten%2C_London_Records_1968_publicity_photo_for_Wikipedia.jpg" width="220" height="266" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Benjamin_Britten%2C_London_Records_1968_publicity_photo_for_Wikipedia.jpg/330px-Benjamin_Britten%2C_London_Records_1968_publicity_photo_for_Wikipedia.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Benjamin_Britten%2C_London_Records_1968_publicity_photo_for_Wikipedia.jpg/440px-Benjamin_Britten%2C_London_Records_1968_publicity_photo_for_Wikipedia.jpg 2x" data-file-width="725" data-file-height="878" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Benjamin_Britten,_London_Records_1968_publicity_photo_for_Wikipedia.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Britten in the mid-1960s, by <a href="/wiki/Hans_Wild" title="Hans Wild">Hans Wild</a></div></div></div>\n<p><b>Edward Benjamin Britten, Baron Britten of Aldeburgh</b> <span class="noexcerpt" style="font-size:85%;"><a href="/wiki/Member_of_the_Order_of_Merit" class="mw-redirect" title="Member of the Order of Merit">OM</a>&#32;<a href="/wiki/Companion_of_Honour" class="mw-redirect" title="Companion of Honour">CH</a></span> (22 November 1913&#160;\xe2\x80\x93 4 December 1976) was an English composer, conductor and pianist. He was a central figure of 20th-century British classical music, with a range of works including opera, other vocal music, orchestral and chamber pieces. His best-known works include the opera <i><a href="/wiki/Peter_Grimes" title="Peter Grimes">Peter Grimes</a></i> (1945), the <i><a href="/wiki/War_Requiem" title="War Requiem">War Requiem</a></i> (1962) and the orchestral showpiece <i><a href="/wiki/The_Young_Person%27s_Guide_to_the_Orchestra" title="The Young Person&#39;s Guide to the Orchestra">The Young Person\'s Guide to the Orchestra</a></i> (1945).\n</p><p>Born in <a href="/wiki/Suffolk" title="Suffolk">Suffolk</a>, the son of a dentist, Britten showed talent from an early age. He studied at the <a href="/wiki/Royal_College_of_Music" title="Royal College of Music">Royal College of Music</a> in London and privately with the composer <a href="/wiki/Frank_Bridge" title="Frank Bridge">Frank Bridge</a>. Britten first came to public attention with the <i><a href="/wiki/A_cappella" title="A cappella">a cappella</a></i> choral work <i><a href="/wiki/A_Boy_was_Born" class="mw-redirect" title="A Boy was Born">A Boy was Born</a></i> in 1934. With the premiere of <i>Peter Grimes</i> in 1945, he leapt to international fame. Over the next 28 years, he wrote 14 more operas, establishing himself as one of the leading 20th-century composers in the genre. In addition to large-scale operas for <a href="/wiki/English_National_Opera" title="English National Opera">Sadler\'s Wells</a> and <a href="/wiki/The_Royal_Opera" title="The Royal Opera">Covent Garden</a>, he wrote "chamber operas" for small forces, suitable for performance in venues of modest size. Among the best known of these is <i><a href="/wiki/The_Turn_of_the_Screw_(opera)" title="The Turn of the Screw (opera)">The Turn of the Screw</a></i> (1954). Recurring themes in his operas include the struggle of an outsider against a hostile society and the corruption of innocence.\n</p><p>Britten\'s other works range from orchestral to choral, solo vocal, chamber and instrumental as well as film music. He took a great interest in writing music for children and amateur performers, including the opera <i><a href="/wiki/Noye%27s_Fludde" title="Noye&#39;s Fludde">Noye\'s Fludde</a></i>, a <i><a href="/wiki/Missa_Brevis_(Britten)" title="Missa Brevis (Britten)">Missa Brevis</a></i>, and the song collection <i><a href="/wiki/Friday_Afternoons" title="Friday Afternoons">Friday Afternoons</a></i>. He often composed with particular performers in mind. His most frequent and important muse was his personal and professional partner, the <a href="/wiki/Tenor_voice" class="mw-redirect" title="Tenor voice">tenor</a> <a href="/wiki/Peter_Pears" title="Peter Pears">Peter Pears</a>; others included <a href="/wiki/Kathleen_Ferrier" title="Kathleen Ferrier">Kathleen Ferrier</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jennifer_Vyvyan" title="Jennifer Vyvyan">Jennifer Vyvyan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Janet_Baker" title="Janet Baker">Janet Baker</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dennis_Brain" title="Dennis Brain">Dennis Brain</a>, <a href="/wiki/Julian_Bream" title="Julian Bream">Julian Bream</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dietrich_Fischer-Dieskau" title="Dietrich Fischer-Dieskau">Dietrich Fischer-Dieskau</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mstislav_Rostropovich" title="Mstislav Rostropovich">Mstislav Rostropovich</a>. Britten was a celebrated pianist and conductor, performing many of his own works in concert and on record. He also performed and recorded works by others, such as <a href="/wiki/Johann_Sebastian_Bach" title="Johann Sebastian Bach">Bach</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Brandenburg_concertos" class="mw-redirect" title="Brandenburg concertos">Brandenburg concertos</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Wolfgang_Amadeus_Mozart" title="Wolfgang Amadeus Mozart">Mozart</a> symphonies, and song cycles by <a href="/wiki/Franz_Schubert" title="Franz Schubert">Schubert</a> and <a href="/wiki/Robert_Schumann" title="Robert Schumann">Schumann</a>.\n</p><p>Together with Pears and the librettist and producer <a href="/wiki/Eric_Crozier" title="Eric Crozier">Eric Crozier</a>, Britten founded the annual <a href="/wiki/Aldeburgh_Festival" title="Aldeburgh Festival">Aldeburgh Festival</a> in 1948, and he was responsible for the creation of <a href="/wiki/Snape_Maltings" title="Snape Maltings">Snape Maltings</a> concert hall in 1967. In his last year, he was the first composer to be given a <a href="/wiki/Life_peer" title="Life peer">life peerage</a>.\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Life_and_career">Life and career</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Benjamin_Britten&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Life and career">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_years">Early years</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Benjamin_Britten&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Early years">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Birthplace_of_Benjamin_Britten.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Birthplace_of_Benjamin_Britten.JPG/220px-Birthplace_of_Benjamin_Britten.JPG" width="220" height="172" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Birthplace_of_Benjamin_Britten.JPG/330px-Birthplace_of_Benjamin_Britten.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Birthplace_of_Benjamin_Britten.JPG/440px-Birthplace_of_Benjamin_Britten.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2507" data-file-height="1963" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Birthplace_of_Benjamin_Britten.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Britten\'s birthplace in Lowestoft, which was the Britten family home for over twenty years</div></div></div>\n<p>Britten was born in the fishing port of <a href="/wiki/Lowestoft" title="Lowestoft">Lowestoft</a> in <a href="/wiki/Suffolk" title="Suffolk">Suffolk</a>, on the east coast of England on 22 November 1913,<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> the feast day of <a href="/wiki/Saint_Cecilia" title="Saint Cecilia">Saint Cecilia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> He was the youngest of four children of Robert Victor Britten (1878\xe2\x80\x931934) and his wife Edith Rhoda, <i>n\xc3\xa9e</i> Hockey (1874\xe2\x80\x931937).<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;n 1&#93;</a></sup> Robert Britten\'s youthful ambition to become a farmer had been thwarted by lack of capital, and he had instead trained as a dentist, a profession he practised successfully but without pleasure. While studying at <a href="/wiki/Charing_Cross_Hospital" title="Charing Cross Hospital">Charing Cross Hospital</a> in London he met Edith Hockey, the daughter of a junior <a href="/wiki/Home_Office" title="Home Office">Home Office</a> official. They were married in September 1901 at <a href="/wiki/St_John%27s,_Smith_Square" title="St John&#39;s, Smith Square">St John\'s, Smith Square</a>, London.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The consensus among biographers of Britten is that his father was a loving but somewhat stern and remote parent.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> Britten, according to his sister Beth, "got on well with him and shared his wry sense of humour, dedication to work and capacity for taking pains".<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Edith Britten was a talented amateur musician and secretary of the Lowestoft Musical Society.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> In the English provinces of the early 20th century, distinctions of social class were taken very seriously. Britten described his family as "very ordinary middle class", but there were aspects of the Brittens that were not ordinary: Edith\'s father was illegitimate, and her mother was an alcoholic; Robert Britten was an agnostic and refused to attend church on Sundays.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> Music was the principal means by which Edith Britten strove to maintain the family\'s social standing, inviting the pillars of the local community to musical soir\xc3\xa9es at the house.<sup id="cite_ref-p7_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-p7-10">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>When Britten was three months old he contracted pneumonia and nearly died.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> The illness left him with a damaged heart,<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> and doctors warned his parents that he would probably never be able to lead a normal life.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> He recovered more fully than expected, and as a boy was a keen tennis player and cricketer.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> To his mother\'s great delight he was an outstandingly musical child, unlike his sisters, who inherited their father\'s indifference to music, while his brother, though musically talented, was interested only in <a href="/wiki/Ragtime" title="Ragtime">ragtime</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> Edith gave the young Britten his first lessons in piano and notation. He made his first attempts at composition when he was five.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> He started piano lessons when he was seven years old, and three years later began to play the <a href="/wiki/Viola" title="Viola">viola</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> He was one of the last composers brought up on exclusively live music: his father refused to have a gramophone or, later, a radio in the house.<sup id="cite_ref-p7_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-p7-10">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Education">Education</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Benjamin_Britten&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Education">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Lowestoft">Lowestoft</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Benjamin_Britten&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Lowestoft">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>When he was seven Britten was sent to a <a href="/wiki/Dame_school" title="Dame school">dame school</a>, run by the Misses Astle. The younger sister, Ethel, gave him piano lessons; in later life he said that he remained grateful for the excellence of her teaching.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> The following year he moved on to his <a href="/wiki/Preparatory_school_(United_Kingdom)" title="Preparatory school (United Kingdom)">prep school</a>, <a href="/wiki/Old_Buckenham_Hall" class="mw-redirect" title="Old Buckenham Hall">South Lodge</a>, Lowestoft, as a <a href="/wiki/Day_pupil" class="mw-redirect" title="Day pupil">day boy</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> The headmaster, Thomas Sewell, was an old-fashioned disciplinarian; the young Britten was outraged at the severe corporal punishments frequently handed out, and later he said that his lifelong <a href="/wiki/Pacifism" title="Pacifism">pacifism</a> probably had its roots in his reaction to the regime at the school.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> He himself rarely fell foul of Sewell, a mathematician, in which subject Britten was a star pupil. The school had no musical tradition, and Britten continued to study the piano with Ethel Astle. From the age of ten he took viola lessons from a friend of his mother, Audrey Alston, who had been a professional player before her marriage.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> In his spare time he composed prolifically. When his <i><a href="/wiki/Simple_Symphony" title="Simple Symphony">Simple Symphony</a></i>, based on these juvenilia, was recorded in 1956, Britten wrote this pen-portrait of his young self for the sleeve note:\n</p>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303468">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Once upon a time there was a prep-school boy.&#160;... He was quite an ordinary little boy&#160;... he loved cricket, only quite liked football (although he kicked a pretty "corner"); he adored mathematics, got on all right with history, was scared by Latin Unseen; he behaved fairly well, only ragged the recognised amount, so that his contacts with the cane or the slipper were happily rare (although one nocturnal expedition to stalk ghosts left its marks behind); he worked his way up the school slowly and steadily, until at the age of thirteen he reached that pinnacle of importance and grandeur never to be quite equalled in later days: the head of the Sixth, head-prefect, and Victor Ludorum. But \xe2\x80\x93 there was one curious thing about this boy: he wrote music. His friends bore with it, his enemies kicked a bit but not for long (he was quite tough), the staff couldn\'t object if his work and games didn\'t suffer. He wrote lots of it, reams and reams of it.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>\n</p></blockquote>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Frank-bridge-1921.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/63/Frank-bridge-1921.jpg/170px-Frank-bridge-1921.jpg" width="170" height="181" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/63/Frank-bridge-1921.jpg/255px-Frank-bridge-1921.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/6/63/Frank-bridge-1921.jpg 2x" data-file-width="300" data-file-height="320" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Frank-bridge-1921.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Frank Bridge, Britten\'s teacher</div></div></div>\n<p>Audrey Alston encouraged Britten to go to symphony concerts in <a href="/wiki/Norwich" title="Norwich">Norwich</a>. At one of these, during the triennial <a href="/wiki/Norfolk_and_Norwich_Festival" title="Norfolk and Norwich Festival">Norfolk and Norwich Festival</a> in October 1924,  he heard <a href="/wiki/Frank_Bridge" title="Frank Bridge">Frank Bridge</a>\'s orchestral poem <i><a href="/wiki/The_Sea_(Bridge)" title="The Sea (Bridge)">The Sea</a></i>, conducted by the composer. It was the first substantial piece of modern music he had ever encountered, and he was, in his own phrase, "knocked sideways" by it.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-modern_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-modern-24">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> Audrey Alston was a friend of Bridge; when he returned to Norwich for the next festival in 1927 she brought her not quite 14-year-old pupil to meet him. Bridge was impressed with the boy, and after they had gone through some of Britten\'s compositions together he invited him to come to London to take lessons from him.<sup id="cite_ref-M8_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-M8-25">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> Robert Britten, supported by Thomas Sewell, doubted the wisdom of pursuing a composing career; a compromise was agreed by which Britten would, as planned, go on to his <a href="/wiki/Public_school_(United_Kingdom)" title="Public school (United Kingdom)">public school</a> the following year but would make regular day-trips to London to study composition with Bridge and piano with his colleague <a href="/wiki/Harold_Samuel" title="Harold Samuel">Harold Samuel</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Bridge impressed on Britten the importance of scrupulous attention to the technical craft of composing<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;n 2&#93;</a></sup> and the maxim that "you should find yourself and be true to what you found."<sup id="cite_ref-dnb_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dnb-29">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> The earliest substantial works Britten composed while studying with Bridge are the String Quartet in F, completed in April 1928, and the <i>Quatre Chansons Fran\xc3\xa7aises</i>, a song-cycle for high voice and orchestra. Authorities differ on the extent of Bridge\'s influence on his pupil\'s technique. <a href="/wiki/Humphrey_Carpenter" title="Humphrey Carpenter">Humphrey Carpenter</a> and <a href="/wiki/Michael_Oliver_(writer,_broadcaster)" title="Michael Oliver (writer, broadcaster)">Michael Oliver</a> judge that Britten\'s abilities as an orchestrator were essentially self-taught;<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Donald_Mitchell_(writer)" title="Donald Mitchell (writer)">Donald Mitchell</a> considers that Bridge had an important influence on the cycle.<sup id="cite_ref-dnb_29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dnb-29">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Public_school_and_Royal_College_of_Music">Public school and Royal College of Music</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Benjamin_Britten&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Public school and Royal College of Music">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Mahler-Ireland-Stravinsky-Shostakovich.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/3/3b/Mahler-Ireland-Stravinsky-Shostakovich.jpg/200px-Mahler-Ireland-Stravinsky-Shostakovich.jpg" width="200" height="200" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/3/3b/Mahler-Ireland-Stravinsky-Shostakovich.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="300" data-file-height="300" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Mahler-Ireland-Stravinsky-Shostakovich.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Early influences, clockwise from top left: <a href="/wiki/Gustav_Mahler" title="Gustav Mahler">Mahler</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Ireland_(composer)" title="John Ireland (composer)">Ireland</a>,<a href="/wiki/Dmitri_Shostakovich" title="Dmitri Shostakovich">Shostakovich,</a> <a href="/wiki/Igor_Stravinsky" title="Igor Stravinsky">Stravinsky</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>In September 1928 Britten went as a <a href="/wiki/Boarding_school" title="Boarding school">boarder</a> to <a href="/wiki/Gresham%27s_School,_Holt" class="mw-redirect" title="Gresham&#39;s School, Holt">Gresham\'s School</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Holt,_Norfolk" title="Holt, Norfolk">Holt, Norfolk</a>. At the time he felt unhappy there, even writing in his diary of contemplating suicide or running away:<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> he hated being separated from his family, most particularly from his mother; he despised the music master; and he was shocked at the prevalence of bullying, though he was not the target of it.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;n 3&#93;</a></sup> He remained there for two years and in 1930, he won a composition scholarship at the <a href="/wiki/Royal_College_of_Music" title="Royal College of Music">Royal College of Music</a> (RCM) in London; his examiners were the composers <a href="/wiki/John_Ireland_(composer)" title="John Ireland (composer)">John Ireland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ralph_Vaughan_Williams" title="Ralph Vaughan Williams">Ralph Vaughan Williams</a> and the college\'s harmony and counterpoint teacher, S P Waddington.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Britten was at the RCM from 1930 to 1933, studying composition with Ireland and piano with <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Benjamin" title="Arthur Benjamin">Arthur Benjamin</a>. He won the <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Sullivan" title="Arthur Sullivan">Sullivan</a> Prize for composition, the <a href="/wiki/Walter_Willson_Cobbett" title="Walter Willson Cobbett">Cobbett</a> Prize for chamber music, and was twice winner of the <a href="/wiki/Ernest_Farrar" title="Ernest Farrar">Ernest Farrar</a> Prize for composition.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> Despite these honours, he was not greatly impressed by the establishment: he found his fellow-students "amateurish and folksy" and the staff "inclined to suspect technical brilliance of being superficial and insincere".<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;n 4&#93;</a></sup> Another Ireland pupil, the composer <a href="/wiki/Humphrey_Searle" title="Humphrey Searle">Humphrey Searle</a>, said that Ireland could be "an inspiring teacher to those on his own wavelength"; Britten was not, and learned little from him.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> He continued to study privately with Bridge, although he later praised Ireland for "nurs[ing] me very gently through a very, very difficult musical adolescence".<sup id="cite_ref-c40_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-c40-41">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Britten also used his time in London to attend concerts and become better acquainted with the music of <a href="/wiki/Igor_Stravinsky" title="Igor Stravinsky">Stravinsky</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dmitri_Shostakovich" title="Dmitri Shostakovich">Shostakovich</a> and, most particularly, <a href="/wiki/Gustav_Mahler" title="Gustav Mahler">Mahler</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;n 5&#93;</a></sup> He intended postgraduate study in Vienna with <a href="/wiki/Alban_Berg" title="Alban Berg">Alban Berg</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arnold_Schoenberg" title="Arnold Schoenberg">Arnold Schoenberg</a>\'s student, but was eventually dissuaded by his parents, on the advice of the RCM staff.<sup id="cite_ref-White1516_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-White1516-44">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The first of Britten\'s compositions to attract wide attention were composed while at the RCM: the <a href="/wiki/Sinfonietta_(Britten)" title="Sinfonietta (Britten)">Sinfonietta, Op. 1</a> (1932), and a set of choral variations <i><a href="/wiki/A_Boy_was_Born" class="mw-redirect" title="A Boy was Born">A Boy was Born</a></i>, written in 1933 for the <a href="/wiki/BBC_Singers" title="BBC Singers">BBC Singers</a>, who first performed it the following year.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> In this same period he wrote <i>Friday Afternoons</i>, a collection of 12 songs for the pupils of Clive House School, <a href="/wiki/Prestatyn" title="Prestatyn">Prestatyn</a>, where his brother was headmaster.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_professional_life">Early professional life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Benjamin_Britten&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Early professional life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In February 1935, at Bridge\'s instigation, Britten was invited to a job interview by the <a href="/wiki/BBC" title="BBC">BBC</a>\'s director of music <a href="/wiki/Adrian_Boult" title="Adrian Boult">Adrian Boult</a> and his assistant <a href="/wiki/Edward_Clark_(conductor)" title="Edward Clark (conductor)">Edward Clark</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> Britten was not enthusiastic about the prospect of working full-time in the BBC music department and was relieved when what came out of the interview was an invitation to write the score for a documentary film, <i><a href="/wiki/The_King%27s_Stamp" title="The King&#39;s Stamp">The King\'s Stamp</a></i>, directed by <a href="/wiki/Alberto_Cavalcanti" title="Alberto Cavalcanti">Alberto Cavalcanti</a> for the <a href="/wiki/GPO_Film_Unit" title="GPO Film Unit">GPO Film Unit</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:122px;"><a href="/wiki/File:AudenVanVechten1939.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/AudenVanVechten1939.jpg/120px-AudenVanVechten1939.jpg" width="120" height="205" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/AudenVanVechten1939.jpg/180px-AudenVanVechten1939.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/AudenVanVechten1939.jpg/240px-AudenVanVechten1939.jpg 2x" data-file-width="373" data-file-height="636" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:AudenVanVechten1939.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>W. H. Auden in 1939</div></div></div>\n<p>Britten became a member of the film unit\'s small group of regular contributors, another of whom was <a href="/wiki/W._H._Auden" title="W. H. Auden">W. H. Auden</a>. Together they worked on the documentary films <i>Coal Face</i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Night_Mail" title="Night Mail">Night Mail</a></i> in 1935.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> They also collaborated on the song cycle <i><a href="/wiki/Our_Hunting_Fathers" title="Our Hunting Fathers">Our Hunting Fathers</a></i> (1936), radical both in politics and musical treatment, and subsequently other works including <i>Cabaret Songs</i>, <i><a href="/wiki/On_This_Island" title="On This Island">On This Island</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Paul_Bunyan_(operetta)" title="Paul Bunyan (operetta)"><i>Paul Bunyan</i></a> and <i><a href="/wiki/Hymn_to_St._Cecilia" class="mw-redirect" title="Hymn to St. Cecilia">Hymn to St. Cecilia</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> Auden was a considerable influence on Britten, encouraging him to widen his aesthetic, intellectual and political horizons, and also to come to terms with his homosexuality. Auden was, as <a href="/wiki/David_Matthews_(composer)" title="David Matthews (composer)">David Matthews</a> puts it, "cheerfully and guiltlessly promiscuous"; Britten, puritanical and conventional by nature, was sexually repressed.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the three years from 1935 to 1937 Britten wrote nearly 40 scores for the theatre, cinema and radio.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> Among the film music of the late 1930s Matthews singles out <i>Night Mail</i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Love_from_a_Stranger_(1937_film)" title="Love from a Stranger (1937 film)">Love from a Stranger</a></i> (1937); from the theatre music he selects for mention  <i><a href="/wiki/The_Ascent_of_F6" title="The Ascent of F6">The Ascent of F6</a></i> (1936), <i><a href="/wiki/On_the_Frontier" title="On the Frontier">On the Frontier</a></i> (1938) and <i><a href="/wiki/Johnson_Over_Jordan" title="Johnson Over Jordan">Johnson Over Jordan</a></i> (1939); and of the music for radio, <i>King Arthur</i> (1937) and <i>The Sword in the Stone</i> (1939).<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1937 there were two events of huge importance in Britten\'s life: his mother died, and he met the <a href="/wiki/Tenor" title="Tenor">tenor</a> <a href="/wiki/Peter_Pears" title="Peter Pears">Peter Pears</a>. Although Britten was extraordinarily devoted to his mother and was devastated at her death, it also seems to have been something of a liberation for him.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> Only after that did he begin to engage in emotional relationships with people his own age or younger.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> Later in the year he got to know Pears while they were both helping to clear out the country cottage of a mutual friend who had died in an air crash.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> Pears quickly became Britten\'s musical inspiration and close (though for the moment platonic) friend. Britten\'s first work for him was composed within weeks of their meeting, <a href="/wiki/The_Company_of_Heaven#History" title="The Company of Heaven">a setting</a> of <a href="/wiki/Emily_Bront%C3%AB" title="Emily Bront\xc3\xab">Emily Bront\xc3\xab</a>\'s poem, "A thousand gleaming fires", for tenor and strings.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>During 1937 Britten composed a <i>Pacifist March</i> to words by <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Duncan" title="Ronald Duncan">Ronald Duncan</a> for the <a href="/wiki/Peace_Pledge_Union" title="Peace Pledge Union">Peace Pledge Union</a>, of which, as a pacifist, he had become an active member; the work was not a success and was soon withdrawn.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> The best known of his compositions from this period is probably <i><a href="/wiki/Variations_on_a_Theme_of_Frank_Bridge" title="Variations on a Theme of Frank Bridge">Variations on a Theme of Frank Bridge</a></i> for string orchestra, described by Matthews as the first of Britten\'s works to become a popular classic.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> It was a success in North America, with performances in Toronto, New York, Boston, Chicago and San Francisco, under conductors including <a href="/wiki/John_Barbirolli" title="John Barbirolli">John Barbirolli</a> and <a href="/wiki/Serge_Koussevitzky" title="Serge Koussevitzky">Serge Koussevitzky</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-press_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-press-60">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="America_1939.E2.80.9342"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="America_1939\xe2\x80\x9342">America 1939\xe2\x80\x9342</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Benjamin_Britten&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: America 1939\xe2\x80\x9342">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In April 1939 Britten and Pears sailed to North America, going first to Canada and then to New York. They had several reasons for leaving England, including the difficult position of pacifists in an increasingly bellicose Europe; the success that Frank Bridge had enjoyed in the US; the departure of Auden and his friend <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Isherwood" title="Christopher Isherwood">Christopher Isherwood</a> to the US from England three months previously; hostile or belittling reviews of Britten\'s music in the English press; and under-rehearsed and inadequate performances.<sup id="cite_ref-dnb_29-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dnb-29">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-grove_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-grove-61">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> Britten and Pears consummated their relationship and from then until Britten\'s death they were partners in both their professional and personal lives.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> When the Second World War began, Britten and Pears turned for advice to the British embassy in Washington and were told that they should remain in the US as artistic ambassadors.<sup id="cite_ref-press_60-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-press-60">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> Pears was inclined to disregard the advice and go back to England; Britten also felt the urge to return, but accepted the embassy\'s counsel and persuaded Pears to do the same.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Already a friend of the composer <a href="/wiki/Aaron_Copland" title="Aaron Copland">Aaron Copland</a>, Britten encountered his latest works <i><a href="/wiki/Billy_the_Kid" title="Billy the Kid">Billy the Kid</a></i> and <i>An Outdoor Overture</i>, both of which influenced his own music.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> In 1940 Britten composed <i>Seven Sonnets of Michelangelo</i>, the first of many song cycles for Pears.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> Britten\'s orchestral works from this period include the <a href="/wiki/Violin_Concerto_(Britten)" title="Violin Concerto (Britten)">Violin Concerto</a> and <i><a href="/wiki/Sinfonia_da_Requiem" title="Sinfonia da Requiem">Sinfonia da Requiem</a></i>. In 1941 Britten produced his first music drama, <i>Paul Bunyan</i>, an <a href="/wiki/Operetta" title="Operetta">operetta</a>, to a <a href="/wiki/Libretto" title="Libretto">libretto</a> by Auden.<sup id="cite_ref-grove_61-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-grove-61">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> While in the US, Britten had his first encounter with <a href="/wiki/Balinese_gamelan" class="mw-redirect" title="Balinese gamelan">Balinese gamelan</a> music, through transcriptions for piano duo made by the Canadian composer <a href="/wiki/Colin_McPhee" title="Colin McPhee">Colin McPhee</a>. The two met in the summer of 1939 and subsequently performed a number of McPhee\'s transcriptions for a recording.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> This musical encounter bore fruit in several Balinese-inspired works later in Britten\'s career.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Moving to the US did not relieve Britten of the nuisance of hostile criticism: although <a href="/wiki/Olin_Downes" title="Olin Downes">Olin Downes</a>, the doyen of New York music critics, and <a href="/wiki/Irving_Kolodin" title="Irving Kolodin">Irving Kolodin</a> took to Britten\'s music, <a href="/wiki/Virgil_Thomson" title="Virgil Thomson">Virgil Thomson</a> was, as the music scholar Suzanne Robinson puts it, consistently "severe and spiteful". Thomson described <i><a href="/wiki/Les_Illuminations_(Britten)" title="Les Illuminations (Britten)">Les Illuminations</a></i> (1940) as "little more than a series of bromidic and facile \'effects\'&#160;... pretentious, banal and utterly disappointing", and was equally unflattering about Pears\'s voice. Robinson surmises that Thomson was motivated by "a mixture of spite, national pride, and professional jealousy".<sup id="cite_ref-press_60-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-press-60">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> <i>Paul Bunyan</i> met with wholesale critical disapproval,<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> and the <i>Sinfonia da Requiem</i> (already rejected by its Japanese sponsors because of its overtly Christian nature) received a mixed reception when Barbirolli and the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Philharmonic" title="New York Philharmonic">New York Philharmonic</a> premiered it in March 1941. The reputation of the work was much enhanced when Koussevitzky took it up shortly afterwards.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Return_to_England">Return to England</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Benjamin_Britten&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Return to England">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Peter-grimes-the-borough-1812.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Peter-grimes-the-borough-1812.jpg/220px-Peter-grimes-the-borough-1812.jpg" width="220" height="359" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/3e/Peter-grimes-the-borough-1812.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="317" data-file-height="518" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Peter-grimes-the-borough-1812.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Page from "Peter Grimes" in 1812 edition of <a href="/wiki/George_Crabbe" title="George Crabbe">Crabbe</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Borough_(George_Crabbe_poem)" title="The Borough (George Crabbe poem)">The Borough</a></i></div></div></div>\n<p>In 1942 Britten read the work of the poet <a href="/wiki/George_Crabbe" title="George Crabbe">George Crabbe</a> for the first time.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/The_Borough_(George_Crabbe_poem)" title="The Borough (George Crabbe poem)">The Borough</a></i>, set on the Suffolk coast close to Britten\'s homeland, awakened in him such longings for England that he knew he must return. He also knew that he must write an opera based on Crabbe\'s poem about the fisherman Peter Grimes.<sup id="cite_ref-press_60-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-press-60">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> Before Britten left the US, Koussevitzky, always generous in encouraging new talent, offered him a $1,000 commission to write the opera.<sup id="cite_ref-press_60-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-press-60">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;n 6&#93;</a></sup> Britten and Pears returned to England in April 1942. During the long transatlantic sea crossing Britten completed the choral works <i><a href="/wiki/A_Ceremony_of_Carols" title="A Ceremony of Carols">A Ceremony of Carols</a></i> and <i>Hymn to St Cecilia</i>. The latter was his last large-scale collaboration with Auden. Britten had grown away from him, and Auden became one of the composer\'s so-called "corpses" \xe2\x80\x93 former intimates from whom he completely cut off contact once they had outlived their usefulness to him or offended him in some way.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Having arrived in Britain, Britten and Pears applied for recognition as <a href="/wiki/Conscientious_objector" title="Conscientious objector">conscientious objectors</a>; Britten was initially allowed only non-combatant service in the military, but on appeal he gained unconditional exemption.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> After the death of his mother in 1937 he had used money she bequeathed him to buy the Old Mill in <a href="/wiki/Snape,_Suffolk" title="Snape, Suffolk">Snape</a>, Suffolk which became his country home. He spent much of his time there in 1944 working on the opera <i><a href="/wiki/Peter_Grimes" title="Peter Grimes">Peter Grimes</a></i>. Pears joined <a href="/wiki/English_National_Opera" title="English National Opera">Sadler\'s Wells Opera Company</a>, whose artistic director, the singer <a href="/wiki/Joan_Cross" title="Joan Cross">Joan Cross</a>, announced her intention to re-open the company\'s home base in London with Britten\'s opera, casting herself and Pears in the leading roles.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;n 7&#93;</a></sup> There were complaints from company members about supposed favouritism and the "cacophony" of Britten\'s score, as well as some ill-suppressed <a href="/wiki/Homophobia" title="Homophobia">homophobic</a> remarks.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> <i>Peter Grimes</i> opened in June 1945 and was hailed by public and critics;<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> its box-office takings matched or exceeded those for <i><a href="/wiki/La_boh%C3%A8me" title="La boh\xc3\xa8me">La boh\xc3\xa8me</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Madama_Butterfly" title="Madama Butterfly">Madame Butterfly</a></i>, which were staged during the same season.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> The opera administrator <a href="/wiki/George_Lascelles,_7th_Earl_of_Harewood" title="George Lascelles, 7th Earl of Harewood">Lord Harewood</a> called it "the first genuinely successful British opera, <a href="/wiki/Gilbert_and_Sullivan" title="Gilbert and Sullivan">Gilbert and Sullivan</a> apart, since <a href="/wiki/Henry_Purcell" title="Henry Purcell">Purcell</a>."<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> Dismayed by the in-fighting among the company, Cross, Britten and Pears severed their ties with Sadler\'s Wells in December 1945, going on to found what was to become the <a href="/wiki/English_Opera_Group" title="English Opera Group">English Opera Group</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>A month after the opening of <i>Peter Grimes</i>, Britten and <a href="/wiki/Yehudi_Menuhin" title="Yehudi Menuhin">Yehudi Menuhin</a> went to Germany to give recitals to concentration camp survivors.<sup id="cite_ref-Matthews_80_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Matthews_80-83">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> What they saw, at <a href="/wiki/Bergen-Belsen_concentration_camp" title="Bergen-Belsen concentration camp">Belsen</a> most of all, so shocked Britten that he refused to talk about it until towards the end of his life, when he told Pears that it had coloured everything he had written since.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> Colin Matthews comments that the next two works Britten composed after his return, the song-cycle <i>The Holy Sonnets of John Donne</i> and the Second String Quartet, contrast strongly with earlier, lighter-hearted works such as <i>Les Illuminations</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> Britten recovered his <i>joie de vivre</i> for <i><a href="/wiki/The_Young_Person%27s_Guide_to_the_Orchestra" title="The Young Person&#39;s Guide to the Orchestra">The Young Person\'s Guide to the Orchestra</a></i> (1945), written for an educational film, <i>Instruments of the Orchestra</i>, directed by <a href="/wiki/Muir_Mathieson" title="Muir Mathieson">Muir Mathieson</a> and featuring the <a href="/wiki/London_Symphony_Orchestra" title="London Symphony Orchestra">London Symphony Orchestra</a> conducted by <a href="/wiki/Malcolm_Sargent" title="Malcolm Sargent">Malcolm Sargent</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> It became, and remained, his most often played and popular work.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Britten\'s next opera, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Rape_of_Lucretia" title="The Rape of Lucretia">The Rape of Lucretia</a></i>, was presented at the first post-war <a href="/wiki/Glyndebourne_Festival_Opera" title="Glyndebourne Festival Opera">Glyndebourne Festival</a> in 1946. It was then taken on tour to provincial cities under the banner of the "Glyndebourne English Opera Company", an uneasy alliance of Britten and his associates with <a href="/wiki/John_Christie_(opera_manager)" title="John Christie (opera manager)">John Christie</a>, the autocratic proprietor of Glyndebourne.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> The tour lost money heavily, and Christie announced that he would underwrite no more tours.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> Britten and his associates set up the English Opera Group; the librettist <a href="/wiki/Eric_Crozier" title="Eric Crozier">Eric Crozier</a> and the designer <a href="/wiki/John_Piper_(artist)" title="John Piper (artist)">John Piper</a> joined Britten as artistic directors. The group\'s express purpose was to produce and commission new English operas and other works, presenting them throughout the country.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> Britten wrote the comic opera <i><a href="/wiki/Albert_Herring" title="Albert Herring">Albert Herring</a></i> for the group in 1947; while on tour in the new work Pears came up with the idea of mounting a festival in the small Suffolk seaside town of <a href="/wiki/Aldeburgh" title="Aldeburgh">Aldeburgh</a>, where Britten had moved from Snape earlier in the year, and which became his principal place of residence for the rest of his life.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Aldeburgh.3B_the_1950s"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Aldeburgh;_the_1950s">Aldeburgh; the 1950s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Benjamin_Britten&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Aldeburgh; the 1950s">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Aldeburgh_Festival" title="Aldeburgh Festival">Aldeburgh Festival</a> was launched in June 1948, with Britten, Pears and Crozier directing it.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> <i>Albert Herring</i> played at the Jubilee Hall, and Britten\'s new cantata for tenor, chorus and orchestra, <a href="/wiki/Saint_Nicolas_(Britten)" title="Saint Nicolas (Britten)"><i>Saint Nicolas</i></a>, was presented in the parish church.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> The festival was an immediate success and became an annual event that has continued into the 21st century.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> New works by Britten featured in almost every festival until his death in 1976, including the premieres of his operas <i><a href="/wiki/A_Midsummer_Night%27s_Dream_(opera)" title="A Midsummer Night&#39;s Dream (opera)">A Midsummer Night\'s Dream</a></i> at the Jubilee Hall in 1960 and <i><a href="/wiki/Death_in_Venice_(opera)" title="Death in Venice (opera)">Death in Venice</a></i> at <a href="/wiki/Snape_Maltings" title="Snape Maltings">Snape Maltings</a> Concert Hall in 1973.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Benjamin_Britten_memorial_window_..._-_geograph.org.uk_-_1131630.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Benjamin_Britten_memorial_window_..._-_geograph.org.uk_-_1131630.jpg/170px-Benjamin_Britten_memorial_window_..._-_geograph.org.uk_-_1131630.jpg" width="170" height="231" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Benjamin_Britten_memorial_window_..._-_geograph.org.uk_-_1131630.jpg/255px-Benjamin_Britten_memorial_window_..._-_geograph.org.uk_-_1131630.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Benjamin_Britten_memorial_window_..._-_geograph.org.uk_-_1131630.jpg/340px-Benjamin_Britten_memorial_window_..._-_geograph.org.uk_-_1131630.jpg 2x" data-file-width="470" data-file-height="640" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Benjamin_Britten_memorial_window_..._-_geograph.org.uk_-_1131630.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/John_Piper_(artist)" title="John Piper (artist)">John Piper</a>\'s Benjamin Britten memorial window in the Church of St Peter and St Paul, Aldeburgh.</div></div></div>\n<p>Unlike many leading English composers, Britten was not known as a teacher,<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;n 8&#93;</a></sup> but in 1949 he accepted his only private pupil, <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Oldham" title="Arthur Oldham">Arthur Oldham</a>, who studied with him for three years. Oldham made himself useful, acting as musical assistant and arranging <i>Variations on a Theme by Frank Bridge</i> for full orchestra for the <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Ashton" title="Frederick Ashton">Frederick Ashton</a> ballet <i>Le R\xc3\xaave de L\xc3\xa9onor</i> (1949),<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> but he later described the teacher\xe2\x80\x93pupil relationship as "beneficial five per cent to [Britten] and ninety-five per cent to me!"<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Throughout the 1950s Britten continued to write operas. <i><a href="/wiki/Billy_Budd_(opera)" title="Billy Budd (opera)">Billy Budd</a></i> (1951) was well received at its <a href="/wiki/Royal_Opera_House" title="Royal Opera House">Covent Garden</a> premiere and was regarded by reviewers as an advance on <i>Peter Grimes</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Gloriana" title="Gloriana">Gloriana</a></i> (1953), written to mark the <a href="/wiki/Coronation_of_Queen_Elizabeth_II" class="mw-redirect" title="Coronation of Queen Elizabeth II">coronation of Elizabeth II</a>, had a cool reception at the gala premiere in the presence of the Queen and the British <a href="/wiki/The_Establishment" title="The Establishment">Establishment</a> <i>en masse</i>. The downbeat story of <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_I_of_England" title="Elizabeth I of England">Elizabeth I</a> in her decline, and Britten\'s score \xe2\x80\x93 reportedly thought by members of the premiere\'s audience "too modern" for such a gala<sup id="cite_ref-hso_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hso-103">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> \xe2\x80\x93 did not overcome what Matthews calls the "ingrained philistinism" of the ruling classes.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;n 9&#93;</a></sup> Although <i>Gloriana</i> did well at the box office, there were no further productions in Britain for another 13 years.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> It was later recognised as one of Britten\'s finer operas.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/The_Turn_of_the_Screw_(opera)" title="The Turn of the Screw (opera)">The Turn of the Screw</a></i> the following year was an unqualified success;<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> together with <i>Peter Grimes</i> it became, and at 2013 remained, one of the two most frequently performed of Britten\'s operas.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the 1950s the "fervently anti-homosexual" <a href="/wiki/Home_Secretary" title="Home Secretary">Home Secretary</a>, <a href="/wiki/David_Maxwell_Fyfe,_1st_Earl_of_Kilmuir" title="David Maxwell Fyfe, 1st Earl of Kilmuir">Sir David Maxwell Fyfe</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> urged the police to enforce the <a href="/wiki/Victorian_era" title="Victorian era">Victorian</a> laws making homosexual acts illegal.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;n 10&#93;</a></sup> Britten and Pears came under scrutiny; Britten was visited by police officers in 1953 and was so perturbed that he discussed with his assistant <a href="/wiki/Imogen_Holst" title="Imogen Holst">Imogen Holst</a> the possibility that Pears might have to enter a sham marriage (with whom is unclear). In the end nothing was done.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>An increasingly important influence on Britten was the music of the East, an interest that was fostered by a tour there with Pears in 1956, when Britten once again encountered the music of the Balinese gamelan<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> and saw for the first time Japanese <a href="/wiki/Noh" title="Noh">Noh</a> plays, which he called "some of the most wonderful drama I have ever seen."<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> These eastern influences were seen and heard in the ballet <i><a href="/wiki/The_Prince_of_the_Pagodas" title="The Prince of the Pagodas">The Prince of the Pagodas</a></i> (1957) and later in two of the three semi-operatic "Parables for Church Performance": <i><a href="/wiki/Curlew_River" title="Curlew River">Curlew River</a></i> (1964) and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Prodigal_Son_(Britten)" title="The Prodigal Son (Britten)">The Prodigal Son</a></i> (1968).<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1960s">1960s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Benjamin_Britten&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: 1960s">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>By the 1960s, the Aldeburgh Festival was outgrowing its customary venues, and plans to build a new concert hall in Aldeburgh were not progressing. When redundant Victorian <a href="/wiki/Malthouse" class="mw-redirect" title="Malthouse">maltings</a> buildings in the village of Snape, six miles inland, became available for hire, Britten realised that the largest of them could be converted into a concert hall and opera house. The 830-seat Snape Maltings hall was opened by the Queen at the start of the twentieth Aldeburgh Festival on 2 June 1967; it was immediately hailed as one of the best concert halls in the country.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> The hall was destroyed by fire in 1969, but Britten was determined that it would be rebuilt in time for the following year\'s festival, which it was. The Queen again attended the opening performance in 1970.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:RIAN_archive_25562_Mstislav_Rostropovich_and_Benjamin_Britten_after_a_concert.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/RIAN_archive_25562_Mstislav_Rostropovich_and_Benjamin_Britten_after_a_concert.jpg/170px-RIAN_archive_25562_Mstislav_Rostropovich_and_Benjamin_Britten_after_a_concert.jpg" width="170" height="243" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/RIAN_archive_25562_Mstislav_Rostropovich_and_Benjamin_Britten_after_a_concert.jpg/255px-RIAN_archive_25562_Mstislav_Rostropovich_and_Benjamin_Britten_after_a_concert.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/RIAN_archive_25562_Mstislav_Rostropovich_and_Benjamin_Britten_after_a_concert.jpg/340px-RIAN_archive_25562_Mstislav_Rostropovich_and_Benjamin_Britten_after_a_concert.jpg 2x" data-file-width="715" data-file-height="1024" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:RIAN_archive_25562_Mstislav_Rostropovich_and_Benjamin_Britten_after_a_concert.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Mstislav_Rostropovich" title="Mstislav Rostropovich">Mstislav Rostropovich</a> and Britten, 1964</div></div></div>\n<p>The Maltings gave the festival a venue that could comfortably house large orchestral works and operas. Britten conducted the first performance outside Russia of Shostakovich\'s <a href="/wiki/Symphony_No._14_(Shostakovich)" title="Symphony No. 14 (Shostakovich)">Fourteenth Symphony</a> at Snape in 1970.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> Shostakovich, a friend since 1960, dedicated the symphony to Britten;<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> he was himself the dedicatee of <i>The Prodigal Son</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> Two other Russian musicians who were close to Britten and regularly performed at the festival were the pianist <a href="/wiki/Sviatoslav_Richter" title="Sviatoslav Richter">Sviatoslav Richter</a> and the cellist <a href="/wiki/Mstislav_Rostropovich" title="Mstislav Rostropovich">Mstislav Rostropovich</a>. Britten composed his <a href="/wiki/Cello_suites_(Britten)" title="Cello suites (Britten)">cello suites</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Cello_Symphony_(Britten)" title="Cello Symphony (Britten)">Cello Symphony</a></i> and <a href="/wiki/Cello_Sonata_(Britten)" title="Cello Sonata (Britten)">Cello Sonata</a> for Rostropovich, who premiered them at the Aldeburgh Festival.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>One of the best known of Britten\'s works, the <i><a href="/wiki/War_Requiem" title="War Requiem">War Requiem</a></i>, was premiered in 1962. He had been asked four years earlier to write a work for the consecration of the new <a href="/wiki/Coventry_Cathedral" title="Coventry Cathedral">Coventry Cathedral</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Modern_architecture" title="Modern architecture">modernist</a> building designed by <a href="/wiki/Basil_Spence" title="Basil Spence">Basil Spence</a>. The old cathedral had been left in ruins by an <a href="/wiki/Coventry_Blitz" title="Coventry Blitz">air-raid on the city</a> in 1940 in which hundreds of people died.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> Britten decided that his work would commemorate the dead of both World Wars in a large-scale score for soloists, chorus, chamber ensemble and orchestra. His text interspersed the traditional <a href="/wiki/Requiem" title="Requiem">Requiem Mass</a> with poems by <a href="/wiki/Wilfred_Owen" title="Wilfred Owen">Wilfred Owen</a>. Matthews writes, "With the <i>War Requiem</i> Britten reached the apex of his reputation: it was almost universally hailed as a masterpiece."<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> Shostakovich told Rostropovich that he believed it to be "the greatest work of the twentieth century".<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1967 the BBC commissioned Britten to write an opera specially for television. <i><a href="/wiki/Owen_Wingrave" title="Owen Wingrave">Owen Wingrave</a></i> was based, like <i>The Turn of the Screw</i>, on a ghost story by <a href="/wiki/Henry_James" title="Henry James">Henry James</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-grove_61-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-grove-61">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> By the 1960s Britten found composition much slower than in his prolific youth; he told the 28-year-old composer <a href="/wiki/Nicholas_Maw" title="Nicholas Maw">Nicholas Maw</a>, "Get as much done now as you can, because it gets much, much more difficult as you grow older."<sup id="cite_ref-max_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-max-126">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> He did not complete the score of the new opera until August 1970.<sup id="cite_ref-grove_61-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-grove-61">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> <i>Owen Wingrave</i> was first broadcast in Britain in May 1971, when it was also televised in Austria, Belgium, Denmark, France, Germany, Ireland, the Netherlands, Norway, Sweden, Switzerland, the US and Yugoslavia.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Last_years">Last years</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Benjamin_Britten&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Last years">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In September 1970 Britten asked <a href="/wiki/Myfanwy_Piper" title="Myfanwy Piper">Myfanwy Piper</a>, who had adapted the two Henry James stories for him, to turn another prose story into a libretto. This was <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Mann" title="Thomas Mann">Thomas Mann</a>\'s novella <i><a href="/wiki/Death_in_Venice" title="Death in Venice">Death in Venice</a></i>, a subject he had been considering for some time.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> At an early stage in composition Britten was told by his doctors that a heart operation was essential if he was to live for more than two years. He was determined to finish the opera and worked urgently to complete it before going into hospital for surgery.<sup id="cite_ref-graham_129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-graham-129">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> His long-term colleague <a href="/wiki/Colin_Graham" title="Colin Graham">Colin Graham</a> wrote:\n</p>\n<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Perhaps of all his works, this one went deepest into Britten\'s own soul: there are extraordinary cross-currents of affinity between himself, his own state of health and mind, Thomas Mann, Aschenbach (Mann\'s dying protagonist), and Peter Pears, who must have had to tear himself in three in order to reconstitute himself as the principal character.<sup id="cite_ref-graham_129-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-graham-129">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup>\n</p></blockquote>\n<p>After the completion of the opera Britten went into the <a href="/wiki/The_Heart_Hospital" class="mw-redirect" title="The Heart Hospital">National Heart Hospital</a> and was operated on in May 1973 to replace a failing heart valve. The replacement was successful, but he suffered a slight stroke, affecting his right hand. This brought his career as a performer to an end.<sup id="cite_ref-grove_61-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-grove-61">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> While in hospital Britten became friendly with a senior nursing sister, Rita Thomson; she moved to Aldeburgh in 1974 and looked after him until his death.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Britten\'s last works include the <i>Suite on English Folk Tunes "A Time There Was"</i> (1974); the Third String Quartet (1975), which drew on material from <i>Death in Venice</i>; and the dramatic cantata <i><a href="/wiki/Phaedra_(cantata)" title="Phaedra (cantata)">Phaedra</a></i> (1975), written for <a href="/wiki/Janet_Baker" title="Janet Baker">Janet Baker</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In June <a href="/wiki/1976_Birthday_Honours" title="1976 Birthday Honours">1976</a>, the last year of his life, Britten accepted a <a href="/wiki/Life_peer" title="Life peer">life peerage</a> \xe2\x80\x93 the first composer so honoured \xe2\x80\x93 becoming Baron Britten, of Aldeburgh in the County of Suffolk.<sup id="cite_ref-TheLondon_132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TheLondon-132">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">&#91;n 11&#93;</a></sup> After the 1976 Aldeburgh Festival, Britten and Pears travelled to Norway, where Britten began writing <i>Praise We Great Men</i>, for voices and orchestra based on a poem by <a href="/wiki/Edith_Sitwell" title="Edith Sitwell">Edith Sitwell</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> He returned to Aldeburgh in August, and wrote <i>Welcome Ode</i> for children\'s choir and orchestra.<sup id="cite_ref-Kennedy114_137-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kennedy114-137">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> In November, Britten realised that he could no longer compose.<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> On his 63rd birthday, 22 November, at his request Rita Thomson organised a champagne party and invited his friends and his sisters Barbara and Beth, to say their goodbyes to the dying composer.<sup id="cite_ref-Matthews155_139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Matthews155-139">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> When Rostropovich made his farewell visit a few days later, Britten gave him what he had written of <i>Praise We Great Men</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Matthews155_139-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Matthews155-139">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="quotebox pullquote floatright" style="width:40%;&#32;; background-color: #D8D8D8;"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303512">.mw-parser-output .quotebox{background-color:#F9F9F9;border:1px solid #aaa;box-sizing:border-box;padding:10px;font-size:88%}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft{margin:0.5em 1.4em 0.8em 0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright{margin:0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.centered{margin:0.5em auto 0.8em auto}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft p,.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright p{font-style:inherit}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-title{background-color:#F9F9F9;text-align:center;font-size:larger;font-weight:bold}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:before{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" \xe2\x80\x9c ";vertical-align:-45%;line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:after{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" \xe2\x80\x9d ";line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .left-aligned{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .right-aligned{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .center-aligned{text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .quotebox cite{display:block;font-style:normal}@media screen and (max-width:360px){.mw-parser-output .quotebox{min-width:100%;margin:0 0 0.8em!important;float:none!important}}</style>\n<div class="quotebox-quote quoted left-aligned" style="">I heard of his death&#160;... and took a long walk in total silence through gently falling snow across a frozen lake, which corresponded exactly to the inexpressible sense of numbness at such a loss. The world is colder and lonelier without the presence of our supreme creator of music.</div>\n<p><cite class="right-aligned" style=""><a href="/wiki/Peter_Maxwell_Davies" title="Peter Maxwell Davies">Peter Maxwell Davies</a>, 1977<sup id="cite_ref-max_126-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-max-126">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup></cite>\n</p>\n</div>\n<p>Britten died of <a href="/wiki/Congestive_heart_failure" class="mw-redirect" title="Congestive heart failure">congestive heart failure</a> on 4 December 1976. His funeral service was held at <a href="/wiki/St_Peter_and_St_Paul%27s_Church,_Aldeburgh" title="St Peter and St Paul&#39;s Church, Aldeburgh">Aldeburgh Parish Church</a> three days later,<sup id="cite_ref-Matthews155_139-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Matthews155-139">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> and he was buried in its churchyard, with a gravestone carved by <a href="/wiki/Reynolds_Stone" title="Reynolds Stone">Reynolds Stone</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> The authorities at <a href="/wiki/Westminster_Abbey" title="Westminster Abbey">Westminster Abbey</a> had offered burial there, but Britten had made it clear that he wished his grave to be side by side with that, in due course, of Pears.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup> A memorial service was held at the Abbey on 10 March 1977, at which the congregation was headed by <a href="/wiki/Queen_Elizabeth_The_Queen_Mother" title="Queen Elizabeth The Queen Mother">Queen Elizabeth The Queen Mother</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Personal_life_and_character">Personal life and character</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Benjamin_Britten&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Personal life and character">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Despite his large number of works on Christian themes, Britten has sometimes been thought of as agnostic.<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> Pears said that when they met in 1937 he was not sure whether or not Britten would have described himself as a Christian.<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> In the 1960s Britten called himself a dedicated Christian, though sympathetic to the radical views propounded by the <a href="/wiki/John_Robinson_(bishop_of_Woolwich)" title="John Robinson (bishop of Woolwich)">Bishop of Woolwich</a> in <i><a href="/wiki/Honest_to_God" title="Honest to God">Honest to God</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup> Politically, Britten was on the left. He told Pears that he always voted either <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Party_(UK)" title="Liberal Party (UK)">Liberal</a> or <a href="/wiki/Labour_Party_(UK)" title="Labour Party (UK)">Labour</a> and could not imagine ever voting <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Party_(UK)" title="Conservative Party (UK)">Conservative</a>, but he was never a member of any party, except the Peace Pledge Union.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Physically, Britten was never robust. He walked and swam regularly and kept himself as fit as he could, but Carpenter in his 1992 biography mentions 20 illnesses, a few of them minor but most fairly serious, suffered over the years by Britten before his final heart complaint developed.<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup> Emotionally, according to some commentators, Britten never completely grew up, retaining in his outlook something of a child\'s view of the world.<sup id="cite_ref-grove_61-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-grove-61">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup> He was not always confident that he was the genius others declared him to be, and though he was hypercritical of his own works, he was acutely, even aggressively sensitive to criticism from anybody else.<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Britten was, as he acknowledged, notorious for dumping friends and colleagues who either offended him or ceased to be of use \xe2\x80\x93 his "corpses".<sup id="cite_ref-k202_150-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-k202-150">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> The conductor <a href="/wiki/Charles_Mackerras" title="Charles Mackerras">Sir Charles Mackerras</a> believed that the term was invented by Lord Harewood. Both Mackerras and Harewood joined the list of corpses, the former for joking that the number of boys in <i>Noye\'s Fludde</i> must have been a delight to the composer, and the latter for an extramarital affair and subsequent divorce from <a href="/wiki/Marion_Stein" title="Marion Stein">Lady Harewood</a>, which shocked the puritanical Britten.<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup> Among other corpses were his librettists <a href="/wiki/Montagu_Slater" title="Montagu Slater">Montagu Slater</a> and Eric Crozier. The latter said in 1949, "He has sometimes told me, jokingly, that one day I would join the ranks of his \'corpses\' and I have always recognized that any ordinary person must soon outlive his usefulness to such a great creative artist as Ben."<sup id="cite_ref-k202_150-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-k202-150">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> Dame Janet Baker said in 1981, "I think he was quite entitled to take what he wanted from others&#160;... He did not want to hurt anyone, but the task in hand was more important than anything or anybody."<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup> Matthews feels that this aspect of Britten has been exaggerated, and he observes that the composer sustained many deep friendships to the end of his life.<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Controversies">Controversies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Benjamin_Britten&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Controversies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Boys">Boys</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Benjamin_Britten&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Boys">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Throughout his adult life, Britten had a particular rapport with children and enjoyed close friendships with several boys, particularly those in their early teens.<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">&#91;n 12&#93;</a></sup> The first such friendship was with Piers Dunkerley, 13 years old in 1934 when Britten was aged 20.<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup> Other boys Britten befriended were the young <a href="/wiki/David_Hemmings" title="David Hemmings">David Hemmings</a> and <a href="/wiki/Michael_Crawford" title="Michael Crawford">Michael Crawford</a>, both of whom sang treble roles in his works in the 1950s.<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup> Hemmings later said, "In all of the time that I spent with him he never abused that trust", and Crawford wrote "I cannot say enough about the kindness of that great man&#160;... he had a wonderful patience and affinity with young people.
