He loved music, and loved youngsters caring about music."<sup id="cite_ref-dnb_29-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dnb-29">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">&#91;n 13&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>It was long suspected by several of Britten\'s close associates that there was something exceptional about his attraction to teenage boys: Auden referred to Britten\'s "attraction to thin-as-a-board juveniles&#160;... to the sexless and innocent",<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup> and Pears once wrote to Britten: "remember there are lovely things in the world still&#160;\xe2\x80\x93 children, boys, sunshine, the sea, Mozart, you and me".<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup> In public, the matter was little discussed during Britten\'s lifetime and much discussed after it.<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">&#91;n 14&#93;</a></sup> Carpenter\'s 1992 biography closely examined the evidence, as do later studies of Britten, most particularly <a href="/wiki/John_Bridcut" title="John Bridcut">John Bridcut</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Britten%27s_Children" title="Britten&#39;s Children">Britten\'s Children</a></i> (2006), which concentrates on Britten\xe2\x80\x99s friendships and relationships with various children and adolescents. Some commentators have continued to question Britten\'s conduct, sometimes very sharply.<sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup> Carpenter and Bridcut conclude that he held any sexual impulses under firm control and kept the relationships affectionate \xe2\x80\x93 including bed-sharing, kissing and skinny dipping \xe2\x80\x93 but strictly platonic.<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Benjamin_Britten_grave_by_Arno_Drucker.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Benjamin_Britten_grave_by_Arno_Drucker.jpg/220px-Benjamin_Britten_grave_by_Arno_Drucker.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Benjamin_Britten_grave_by_Arno_Drucker.jpg/330px-Benjamin_Britten_grave_by_Arno_Drucker.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Benjamin_Britten_grave_by_Arno_Drucker.jpg/440px-Benjamin_Britten_grave_by_Arno_Drucker.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="768" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Benjamin_Britten_grave_by_Arno_Drucker.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Britten\'s grave in St. Peter and St Paul\'s Church, <a href="/wiki/Aldeburgh" title="Aldeburgh">Aldeburgh</a>, Suffolk</div></div></div>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Cause_of_death">Cause of death</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Benjamin_Britten&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Cause of death">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>A more recent controversy was the statement in a 2013 biography of Britten by <a href="/wiki/Paul_Kildea" title="Paul Kildea">Paul Kildea</a> that the composer\'s heart failure was due to undetected <a href="/wiki/Syphilis" title="Syphilis">syphilis</a>, which Kildea speculates was a result of Pears\'s promiscuity while the two were living in New York.<sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup> In response, Britten\'s consultant cardiologist said that, like all the hospital\'s similar cases, Britten was routinely screened for syphilis before the operation, with negative results.<sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup> He described as "complete rubbish" Kildea\'s allegation that the surgeon who operated on Britten in 1973 would or even could have covered up a syphilitic condition.<sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup> Kildea continued to maintain, "When all the composer\'s symptoms are considered there can be only one cause".<sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup> In <i>The Times</i>, <a href="/wiki/Richard_Morrison_(music_critic)" title="Richard Morrison (music critic)">Richard Morrison</a> praised the rest of Kildea\'s book, and hoped that its reputation would not be "tarnished by one sensational speculation&#160;... some second-hand hearsay&#160;... presenting unsubstantiated gossip as fact".<sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Music">Music</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Benjamin_Britten&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Music">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_compositions_by_Benjamin_Britten" title="List of compositions by Benjamin Britten">List of compositions by Benjamin Britten</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Influences">Influences</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Benjamin_Britten&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Influences">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Britten\'s early musical life was dominated by the classical masters; his mother\'s ambition was for him to become the "<a href="/wiki/Three_Bs" title="Three Bs">Fourth B</a>" \xe2\x80\x93 after <a href="/wiki/Johann_Sebastian_Bach" title="Johann Sebastian Bach">Bach</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ludwig_van_Beethoven" title="Ludwig van Beethoven">Beethoven</a> and <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Brahms" title="Johannes Brahms">Brahms</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup> Britten was later to assert that his initial development as a composer was stifled by reverence for these masters: "Between the ages of thirteen and sixteen I knew every note of Beethoven and Brahms. I remember receiving the full score of <i>Fidelio</i> for my fourteenth birthday&#160;... But I think in a sense I never forgave them for having led me astray in my own particular thinking and natural inclinations".<sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup> He developed a particular animosity towards Brahms, whose piano music he had once held in great esteem; in 1952 he confided that he played through all Brahms\'s music from time to time, "to see if I am right about him; I usually find that I underestimated last time how bad it was!"<sup id="cite_ref-grove_61-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-grove-61">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Through his association with Frank Bridge, Britten\'s musical horizons expanded.<sup id="cite_ref-M8_25-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-M8-25">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> He discovered the music of <a href="/wiki/Claude_Debussy" title="Claude Debussy">Debussy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Maurice_Ravel" title="Maurice Ravel">Ravel</a> which, Matthews writes, "gave him a model for an orchestral sound".<sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup> Bridge also led Britten to the music of Schoenberg and Berg; the latter\'s death in 1935 affected Britten deeply. A letter at that time reveals his thoughts on the contemporary music scene: "The real musicians are so few &amp; far between, aren\'t they? Apart from the Bergs, Stravinskys, Schoenbergs &amp; Bridges one is a bit stumped for names, isn\'t one?" \xe2\x80\x93 adding, as an afterthought: "Shostakovitch \xe2\x80\x93 perhaps \xe2\x80\x93 possibly".<sup id="cite_ref-grove_61-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-grove-61">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> By this time Britten had developed a lasting hostility towards the English pastoral school represented by Vaughan Williams and Ireland, whose work he compared unfavourably with the "brilliant folk-song arrangements of Percy Grainger"; <a href="/wiki/Percy_Grainger" title="Percy Grainger">Grainger</a> became the inspiration of many of Britten\'s later folk arrangements.<sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup> Britten was also impressed by <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Delius" title="Frederick Delius">Delius</a>, and thought <i><a href="/wiki/Brigg_Fair#Delius_orchestral_setting" title="Brigg Fair">Brigg Fair</a></i> "delicious" when he heard it in 1931.<sup id="cite_ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup> Also in that year he heard Stravinsky\'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Rite_of_Spring" title="The Rite of Spring">The Rite of Spring</a></i>, which he found "bewildering and terrifying", yet at the same time "incredibly marvellous and arresting". The same composer\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Symphony_of_Psalms" title="Symphony of Psalms">Symphony of Psalms</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Petrushka" title="Petrushka">Petrushka</a></i> were lauded in similar terms.<sup id="cite_ref-grove_61-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-grove-61">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>  However, he and Stravinsky later developed a mutual antipathy informed by jealousy and mistrust.<sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Besides his growing attachments to the works of 20th century masters, Britten \xe2\x80\x93 along with his contemporary <a href="/wiki/Michael_Tippett" title="Michael Tippett">Michael Tippett</a> \xe2\x80\x93 was devoted to the English music of the late 17th and early 18th centuries, in particular the work of Purcell.<sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup> In defining his mission as a composer of opera, Britten wrote: "One of my chief aims is to try to restore to the musical setting of the English Language a brilliance, freedom and vitality that have been curiously rare since the death of Purcell".<sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup> Among the closest of Britten\'s kindred composer spirits \xe2\x80\x93 even more so than Purcell \xe2\x80\x93 was <a href="/wiki/Gustav_Mahler" title="Gustav Mahler">Mahler</a>, whose <a href="/wiki/Symphony_No._4_(Mahler)" title="Symphony No. 4 (Mahler)">Fourth Symphony</a> Britten heard in September 1930. At that time Mahler\'s music was little regarded and rarely played in English concert halls.<sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup> Britten later wrote of how the scoring of this work impressed him: "...&#160;entirely clean and transparent&#160;... the material was remarkable, and the melodic shapes highly original, with such rhythmic and harmonic tension from beginning to end".<sup id="cite_ref-mahler_42-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mahler-42">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> He soon discovered other Mahler works, in particular <i><a href="/wiki/Das_Lied_von_der_Erde" title="Das Lied von der Erde">Das Lied von der Erde</a></i>; he wrote to a friend about the concluding "Abschied" of <i>Das Lied</i>: "It is cruel, you know, that music should be so beautiful".<sup id="cite_ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-183" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-183">&#91;n 15&#93;</a></sup> Apart from Mahler\'s general influence on Britten\'s compositional style, the incorporation by Britten of popular tunes (as, for example, in <i><a href="/wiki/Death_in_Venice_(opera)" title="Death in Venice (opera)">Death in Venice</a></i>) is a direct inheritance from the older composer.<sup id="cite_ref-184" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-184">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Operas">Operas</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Benjamin_Britten&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Operas">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The Britten-Pears Foundation considers the composer\'s operas "perhaps the most substantial and important part of his compositional legacy."<sup id="cite_ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup> Britten\'s operas are firmly established in the international repertoire: according to <a href="/wiki/Operabase" title="Operabase">Operabase</a>, they are performed worldwide more than those of any other composer born in the 20th century,<sup id="cite_ref-obtop_186-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-obtop-186">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup> and only <a href="/wiki/Giacomo_Puccini" title="Giacomo Puccini">Puccini</a> and <a href="/wiki/Richard_Strauss" title="Richard Strauss">Richard Strauss</a> come ahead of him if the list is extended to all operas composed after 1900.<sup id="cite_ref-bpf1103pr_187-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bpf1103pr-187">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The early operetta <i><a href="/wiki/Paul_Bunyan_(operetta)" title="Paul Bunyan (operetta)">Paul Bunyan</a></i> stands apart from Britten\'s later operatic works. Philip Brett, in <i><a href="/wiki/Grove_Dictionary_of_Music_and_Musicians" class="mw-redirect" title="Grove Dictionary of Music and Musicians">Grove</a>\'</i>s article on Britten, calls it "a patronizing attempt by W H Auden to evoke the spirit of a nation not his own in which Britten was a somewhat dazzled accomplice".<sup id="cite_ref-grove_61-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-grove-61">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> The American public liked it, but the critics did not,<sup id="cite_ref-189" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-189">&#91;n 16&#93;</a></sup> and it fell into neglect until interest revived near the end of the composer\'s life.<sup id="cite_ref-grove_61-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-grove-61">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Peter_Pears_publicity_photo_1971_crop_jpeg.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Peter_Pears_publicity_photo_1971_crop_jpeg.jpg/220px-Peter_Pears_publicity_photo_1971_crop_jpeg.jpg" width="220" height="162" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Peter_Pears_publicity_photo_1971_crop_jpeg.jpg/330px-Peter_Pears_publicity_photo_1971_crop_jpeg.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Peter_Pears_publicity_photo_1971_crop_jpeg.jpg/440px-Peter_Pears_publicity_photo_1971_crop_jpeg.jpg 2x" data-file-width="930" data-file-height="685" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Peter_Pears_publicity_photo_1971_crop_jpeg.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Peter Pears as the General in <i><a href="/wiki/Owen_Wingrave" title="Owen Wingrave">Owen Wingrave</a></i>, 1971</div></div></div>\n<p>Britten\'s subsequent operas range from large-scale works written for full-strength opera companies, to chamber operas for performance by small touring opera ensembles or in churches and schools. In the large-scale category are <i><a href="/wiki/Peter_Grimes" title="Peter Grimes">Peter Grimes</a></i> (1945), <i><a href="/wiki/Billy_Budd_(opera)" title="Billy Budd (opera)">Billy Budd</a></i> (1951), <i><a href="/wiki/Gloriana" title="Gloriana">Gloriana</a></i> (1953), <i><a href="/wiki/A_Midsummer_Night%27s_Dream_(opera)" title="A Midsummer Night&#39;s Dream (opera)">A Midsummer Night\'s Dream</a></i> (1960) and <i>Death in Venice</i> (1973). Of the remaining operas, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Rape_of_Lucretia" title="The Rape of Lucretia">The Rape of Lucretia</a></i> (1946), <i><a href="/wiki/Albert_Herring" title="Albert Herring">Albert Herring</a></i> (1947), <i><a href="/wiki/The_Little_Sweep" title="The Little Sweep">The Little Sweep</a></i> (1949) and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Turn_of_the_Screw_(opera)" title="The Turn of the Screw (opera)">The Turn of the Screw</a></i> (1954) were written for small opera companies. <i><a href="/wiki/Noye%27s_Fludde" title="Noye&#39;s Fludde">Noye\'s Fludde</a></i> (1958), <i><a href="/wiki/Curlew_River" title="Curlew River">Curlew River</a></i> (1964), <i><a href="/wiki/The_Burning_Fiery_Furnace" title="The Burning Fiery Furnace">The Burning Fiery Furnace</a></i> (1966) and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Prodigal_Son_(Britten)" title="The Prodigal Son (Britten)">The Prodigal Son</a></i> (1968) were for church performance, and had their premieres at <a href="/wiki/St_Bartholomew%27s_Church,_Orford" title="St Bartholomew&#39;s Church, Orford">St Bartholomew\'s Church, Orford</a>.  The secular <i>The Golden Vanity</i> was intended to be performed in schools. <i><a href="/wiki/Owen_Wingrave" title="Owen Wingrave">Owen Wingrave</a></i>, written for television, was first presented live by the <a href="/wiki/The_Royal_Opera" title="The Royal Opera">Royal Opera</a> at Covent Garden in 1973, two years after its broadcast premiere.<sup id="cite_ref-grove_61-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-grove-61">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Music critics have frequently commented on the recurring theme in Britten\'s operas from <i>Peter Grimes</i> onward of the isolated individual at odds with a hostile society.<sup id="cite_ref-190" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-190">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup> The extent to which this reflected Britten\'s perception of himself, pacifist and homosexual, in the England of the 1930s, 40s and 50s is debated.<sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup> Another recurrent theme is the corruption of innocence, most sharply seen in <i>The Turn of the Screw</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-192" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-192">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Over the 28 years between <i>Peter Grimes</i> and <i>Death in Venice</i> Britten\'s musical style changed, as he introduced elements of <a href="/wiki/Atonalism" class="mw-redirect" title="Atonalism">atonalism</a> \xe2\x80\x93 though remaining essentially a tonal composer \xe2\x80\x93 and of eastern music, particularly gamelan sounds but also eastern harmonies.<sup id="cite_ref-grove_61-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-grove-61">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> In <i>A Midsummer Night\'s Dream</i> the orchestral scoring varies to fit the nature of each set of characters: "the bright, percussive sounds of harps, keyboards and percussion for the fairy world, warm strings and wind for the pairs of lovers, and lower woodwind and brass for the mechanicals".<sup id="cite_ref-193" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-193">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup> In <i>Death in Venice</i> Britten turns Tadzio and his family into silent dancers, "accompanied by the colourful, glittering sounds of tuned percussion to emphasize their remoteness."<sup id="cite_ref-194" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-194">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>As early as 1948 the music analyst <a href="/wiki/Hans_Keller" title="Hans Keller">Hans Keller</a>, summarising Britten\'s impact on 20th-century opera to that date, compared his contribution to that of <a href="/wiki/Wolfgang_Amadeus_Mozart" title="Wolfgang Amadeus Mozart">Mozart</a> in the 18th century: "Mozart may in some respects be regarded as a founder (a \'second founder\') of opera. The same can already be said today, as far as the modern British \xe2\x80\x93 perhaps not only British \xe2\x80\x93 field goes, of Britten".<sup id="cite_ref-195" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-195">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup> In addition to his own original operas, Britten, together with Imogen Holst, extensively revised Purcell\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Dido_and_Aeneas" title="Dido and Aeneas">Dido and Aeneas</a></i> (1951) and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Fairy-Queen" title="The Fairy-Queen">The Fairy-Queen</a></i> (1967). These "realisations" brought Purcell, who was then neglected, to a wider public, but have themselves been neglected since the dominance of the trend to authentic performance practice.<sup id="cite_ref-196" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-196">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup> His 1948 revision of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Beggar%27s_Opera" title="The Beggar&#39;s Opera">The Beggar\'s Opera</a></i> amounts to a wholesale recomposition, retaining the original melodies but giving them new, highly sophisticated orchestral accompaniments.<sup id="cite_ref-197" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-197">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Song_cycles">Song cycles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Benjamin_Britten&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Song cycles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Throughout his career Britten was drawn to the song cycle form. In 1928, when he was 14, he composed an orchestral cycle, <i>Quatre chansons fran\xc3\xa7aises</i>, setting words by <a href="/wiki/Victor_Hugo" title="Victor Hugo">Victor Hugo</a> and <a href="/wiki/Paul_Verlaine" title="Paul Verlaine">Paul Verlaine</a>. Brett comments that though the work is much influenced by Wagner on the one hand and French mannerisms on the other, "the diatonic nursery-like tune for the sad boy with the consumptive mother in \'L\'enfance\' is entirely characteristic".<sup id="cite_ref-grove_61-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-grove-61">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> After he came under Auden\'s influence Britten composed <i>Our Hunting Fathers</i> (1936), ostensibly a protest against fox-hunting but which also alludes allegorically to the contemporary political state of Europe. The work has never been popular; in 1948 the critic Colin Mason lamented its neglect and called it one of Britten\'s greatest works. In Mason\'s view the cycle is "as exciting as <i>Les Illuminations</i>, and offers many interesting and enjoyable foretastes of the best moments of his later works."<sup id="cite_ref-mason1_198-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mason1-198">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Britten-poets.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/2/2c/Britten-poets.jpg" width="200" height="200" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="200" data-file-height="200" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Britten-poets.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Poets whose words Britten set included (clockwise from top l) <a href="/wiki/William_Blake" title="William Blake">Blake</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Rimbaud" title="Arthur Rimbaud">Rimbaud</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wilfred_Owen" title="Wilfred Owen">Owen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Paul_Verlaine" title="Paul Verlaine">Verlaine</a></div></div></div>\n<p>The first of Britten\'s song cycles to gain widespread popularity was <i><a href="/wiki/Les_Illuminations_(Britten)" title="Les Illuminations (Britten)">Les Illuminations</a></i> (1940), for high voice (originally soprano, later more often sung by tenors)<sup id="cite_ref-200" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-200">&#91;n 17&#93;</a></sup> with string orchestra accompaniment, setting words by <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Rimbaud" title="Arthur Rimbaud">Arthur Rimbaud</a>. Britten\'s music reflects the eroticism in Rimbaud\'s poems; Copland commented of the section "Antique" that he did not know how Britten dared to write the melody.<sup id="cite_ref-grove_61-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-grove-61">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> "Antique" was dedicated to "K.H.W.S.", or <a href="/wiki/John_Woolford" title="John Woolford">Wulff Scherchen</a>, Britten\'s first romantic interest. Matthews judges the piece the crowning masterpiece of Britten\'s early years.<sup id="cite_ref-M56_199-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-M56-199">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup> By the time of Britten\'s next cycle, <i><a href="/wiki/Seven_Sonnets_of_Michelangelo" title="Seven Sonnets of Michelangelo">Seven Sonnets of Michelangelo</a></i> (1942) for tenor and piano, Pears had become his partner and muse; in Matthews\'s phrase, Britten wrote the cycle as "his declaration of love for Peter".<sup id="cite_ref-M56_199-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-M56-199">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup> It too finds the sensuality of the verses it sets, though in its structure it resembles a conventional 19th-century song cycle. Mason draws a distinction between this and Britten\'s earlier cycles, because here each song is self-contained, and has no thematic connection with any of the others.<sup id="cite_ref-mason1_198-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mason1-198">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <i><a href="/wiki/Serenade_for_Tenor,_Horn_and_Strings" title="Serenade for Tenor, Horn and Strings">Serenade for Tenor, Horn and Strings</a></i> (1943) sets verses by a variety of poets, all on the theme of night-time. Though Britten described the cycle as "not important stuff, but quite pleasant, I think", it was immediately greeted as a masterpiece, and together with <i>Peter Grimes</i> it established him as one of the leading composers of his day.<sup id="cite_ref-dnb_29-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dnb-29">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> Mason calls it "a beautifully unified work on utterly dissimilar poems, held together by the most superficial but most effective, and therefore most suitable symphonic method. Some of the music is pure <a href="/wiki/Word_painting" title="Word painting">word-painting</a>, some of it mood-painting, of the subtlest kind."<sup id="cite_ref-mason2_201-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mason2-201">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup> Two years later, after witnessing the horrors of Belsen, Britten composed <i><a href="/wiki/The_Holy_Sonnets_of_John_Donne" title="The Holy Sonnets of John Donne">The Holy Sonnets of John Donne</a></i>, a work whose bleakness was not matched until his final tenor and piano cycle a quarter of a century later. Britten\'s technique in this cycle ranges from atonality in the first song to firm tonality later, with a resolute B major chord at the climax of "Death, be not proud".<sup id="cite_ref-Matthews_80_83-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Matthews_80-83">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><i><a href="/wiki/Nocturne_(Britten)" title="Nocturne (Britten)">Nocturne</a></i> (1958) is the last of the orchestral cycles. As in the <i>Serenade</i>, Britten set words by a range of poets, who here include <a href="/wiki/William_Shakespeare" title="William Shakespeare">Shakespeare</a>, <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Taylor_Coleridge" title="Samuel Taylor Coleridge">Coleridge</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Keats" title="John Keats">Keats</a>, <a href="/wiki/Percy_Bysshe_Shelley" title="Percy Bysshe Shelley">Shelley</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alfred,_Lord_Tennyson" title="Alfred, Lord Tennyson">Tennyson</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wilfred_Owen" title="Wilfred Owen">Wilfred Owen</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-grove_61-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-grove-61">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> The whole cycle is darker in tone than the <i>Serenade</i>, with pre-echoes of the <i>War Requiem</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-m120_202-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-m120-202">&#91;185&#93;</a></sup> All the songs have subtly different orchestrations, with a prominent <a href="/wiki/Obbligato" title="Obbligato">obbligato</a> part for a different instrument in each.<sup id="cite_ref-m120_202-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-m120-202">&#91;185&#93;</a></sup>\nAmong Britten\'s later song cycles with piano accompaniment is the <i><a href="/wiki/Songs_and_Proverbs_of_William_Blake" title="Songs and Proverbs of William Blake">Songs and Proverbs of William Blake</a></i>, composed for the baritone <a href="/wiki/Dietrich_Fischer-Dieskau" title="Dietrich Fischer-Dieskau">Dietrich Fischer-Dieskau</a>. This presents all its poems in a continuous stream of music; Brett writes that it "interleaves a ritornello-like setting of the seven proverbs with seven songs that paint an increasingly sombre picture of human existence."<sup id="cite_ref-grove_61-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-grove-61">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> A Pushkin cycle, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Poet%27s_Echo" title="The Poet&#39;s Echo">The Poet\'s Echo</a></i> (1965), was written for <a href="/wiki/Galina_Vishnevskaya" title="Galina Vishnevskaya">Galina Vishnevskaya</a>, and shows a more robust and extrovert side of the composer.<sup id="cite_ref-grove_61-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-grove-61">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> Though written ostensibly in the tradition of European song cycles, it draws atmospherically on the polyphony of south-east Asian music.<sup id="cite_ref-dnb_29-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dnb-29">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Who_Are_These_Children%3F" title="Who Are These Children?">Who Are These Children?</a></i> (1969), setting 12 verses by <a href="/wiki/William_Soutar" title="William Soutar">William Soutar</a>, is among the grimmest of Britten\'s cycles. After he could no longer play the piano, Britten composed a cycle of <a href="/wiki/Robert_Burns" title="Robert Burns">Robert Burns</a> settings, <i><a href="/wiki/A_Birthday_Hansel" title="A Birthday Hansel">A Birthday Hansel</a></i> (1976), for voice and harp.<sup id="cite_ref-grove_61-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-grove-61">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_vocal_works">Other vocal works</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Benjamin_Britten&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Other vocal works">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Nicholas Maw said of Britten\'s vocal music: "His feeling for poetry (not only English) and the inflexions of language make him, I think, the greatest musical realizer of English".<sup id="cite_ref-max_126-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-max-126">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> One of the best-known works in which Britten set poetry was the <i><a href="/wiki/War_Requiem" title="War Requiem">War Requiem</a></i> (1962). It intersperses the Latin <a href="/wiki/Requiem_mass" class="mw-redirect" title="Requiem mass">requiem mass</a>, sung by <a href="/wiki/Soprano" title="Soprano">soprano</a> and chorus, with settings of works by the First World War poet Wilfred Owen, sung by tenor and <a href="/wiki/Baritone" title="Baritone">baritone</a>. At the end the two elements are combined, as the last line of Owen\'s "Strange meeting" mingles with the <i><a href="/wiki/In_paradisum" title="In paradisum">In paradisum</a></i> of the mass. Matthews describes the conclusion of the work as "a great wave of benediction [which] recalls the end of the <i>Sinfonia da Requiem</i>, and its similar ebbing away into the sea that symbolises both reconciliation and death."<sup id="cite_ref-203" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-203">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup> Other works for voices and orchestra include the <i><a href="/wiki/Missa_Brevis_(Britten)" title="Missa Brevis (Britten)">Missa Brevis</a></i> and the <i><a href="/wiki/Cantata_academica" title="Cantata academica">Cantata academica</a></i> (both 1959) on religious themes, and the late cantata <i><a href="/wiki/Phaedra_(cantata)" title="Phaedra (cantata)">Phaedra</a></i> (1975), a story of fated love and death modelled on <a href="/wiki/George_Frideric_Handel" title="George Frideric Handel">Handel</a>\'s Italian cantatas.<sup id="cite_ref-204" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-204">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Smaller-scale works for accompanied voice include the five <a href="/wiki/Five_Canticles" class="mw-redirect" title="Five Canticles">Canticles</a>, composed between 1947 and 1974. They are written for a variety of voices (tenor in all five; counter-tenor or alto in II and IV and baritone in IV) and accompaniments (piano in I to IV, horn in III and harp in V).<sup id="cite_ref-205" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-205">&#91;188&#93;</a></sup> The first is a setting of <a href="/wiki/Francis_Quarles" title="Francis Quarles">Francis Quarles</a>\'s 17th century poem "A Divine Rapture",<sup id="cite_ref-matthews98_206-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-matthews98-206">&#91;189&#93;</a></sup> and according to Britten was modelled on Purcell\'s <i>Divine Hymns</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-207" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-207">&#91;190&#93;</a></sup> Matthews describes it as one of the composer\'s most serene works, which "ends in a mood of untroubled happiness that would soon become rare in Britten\'s music".<sup id="cite_ref-matthews98_206-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-matthews98-206">&#91;189&#93;</a></sup> The second Canticle was written in 1952, between <i>Billy Budd</i> and <i>Gloriana</i>, on the theme of <a href="/wiki/Abraham" title="Abraham">Abraham</a>\'s obedience to Divine Authority in the proffered sacrifice of his son <a href="/wiki/Isaac" title="Isaac">Isaac</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-208" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-208">&#91;191&#93;</a></sup> <sup id="cite_ref-210" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-210">&#91;n 18&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Canticle_III:_Still_falls_the_rain" title="Canticle III: Still falls the rain">"Canticle III"</a> from 1954 is a setting of Edith Sitwell\'s wartime poem "Still Falls the Rain", composed just after <i>The Turn of the Screw</i> with which it is structurally and stylistically associated. The twelve-note cycle in the first five bars of the piano part of the Canticle introduced a feature that became thereafter a regular part of Britten\'s compositional technique.<sup id="cite_ref-211" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-211">&#91;193&#93;</a></sup> The fourth Canticle, premiered in 1971 is based on <a href="/wiki/T._S._Eliot" title="T. S. Eliot">T. S. Eliot</a>\'s poem "Journey of the Magi". It is musically close to <i>The Burning Fiery Furnace</i> of 1966; Matthews refers to it as a "companion piece" to the earlier work.<sup id="cite_ref-212" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-212">&#91;194&#93;</a></sup> The final Canticle was another Eliot setting, his juvenile poem "Death of Saint Narcissus". Although Britten had little idea of what the poem was about,<sup id="cite_ref-213" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-213">&#91;195&#93;</a></sup> the musicologist <a href="/wiki/Arnold_Whittall" title="Arnold Whittall">Arnold Whittall</a> finds the text "almost frighteningly apt&#160;... for a composer conscious of his own sickness".<sup id="cite_ref-214" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-214">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup> Matthews sees Narcissus as "another figure from [Britten\'s] magic world of dreams and ideal beauty".<sup id="cite_ref-215" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-215">&#91;197&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Orchestral_works">Orchestral works</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Benjamin_Britten&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Orchestral works">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The Britten scholar Donald Mitchell has written, "It is easy, because of the scope, stature, and sheer volume of the operas, and the wealth of vocal music of all kinds, to pay insufficient attention to the many works Britten wrote in other, specifically non-vocal genres."<sup id="cite_ref-dnb_29-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dnb-29">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> Maw said of Britten, "He is one of the 20th century\'s great orchestral composers&#160;... His orchestration has an individuality, incisiveness and integration with the musical material only achieved by the greatest composers."<sup id="cite_ref-max_126-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-max-126">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> Among Britten\'s best-known orchestral works are the <i><a href="/wiki/Variations_on_a_Theme_of_Frank_Bridge" title="Variations on a Theme of Frank Bridge">Variations on a Theme of Frank Bridge</a></i> (1937), the <i><a href="/wiki/Sinfonia_da_Requiem" title="Sinfonia da Requiem">Sinfonia da Requiem</a></i> (1940), the <i>Four Sea Interludes</i> (1945) and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Young_Person%27s_Guide_to_the_Orchestra" title="The Young Person&#39;s Guide to the Orchestra">The Young Person\'s Guide to the Orchestra</a></i> (1945). The Variations, an affectionate tribute to Britten\'s teacher, range from comic parodies of Italian operatic clich\xc3\xa9s and Viennese waltzes to a strutting march, reflecting the rise of militarism in Europe, and a Mahlerian funeral march; the piece ends with an exuberant <a href="/wiki/Fugue" title="Fugue">fugal</a> finale.<sup id="cite_ref-216" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-216">&#91;198&#93;</a></sup> The Sinfonia moves from an opening <i>Lacrymosa</i> filled with fear and lamentation to a fierce <a href="/wiki/Dies_irae" title="Dies irae">Dies irae</a> and then to a final <i>Requiem aeternam</i>, described by the critic Herbert Glass as "the most uneasy \'eternal rest\' possible".<sup id="cite_ref-217" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-217">&#91;199&#93;</a></sup> Mason considers the Sinfonia a failure: "less entertaining than usual, because its object is not principally to entertain but to express symphonically. It fails because it is neither picturesquely nor formally symphonic".<sup id="cite_ref-mason1_198-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mason1-198">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup> The <i>Sea Interludes</i>, adapted by Britten from the full score of <i>Peter Grimes</i>, make a concert suite depicting the sea and the Borough in which the opera is set; the character of the music is strongly contrasted between "Dawn", "Sunday Morning", "Moonlight" and "Storm". The commentator Howard Posner observes that there is not a bar in the interludes, no matter how beautiful, that is free of foreboding.<sup id="cite_ref-218" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-218">&#91;200&#93;</a></sup> <i>The Young Person\'s Guide</i>, based on a theme by Purcell, showcases the orchestra\'s individual sections and groups, and gained widespread popularity from the outset.<sup id="cite_ref-headington82_219-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-headington82-219">&#91;201&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Matthews85_220-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Matthews85-220">&#91;202&#93;</a></sup> Christopher Headington calls the work "exuberant and uncomplicated music, scored with clarity and vigour [that] fits well into Britten\'s <i>oeuvre</i>."<sup id="cite_ref-headington82_219-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-headington82-219">&#91;201&#93;</a></sup> David Matthews calls it "a brilliant educational exercise."<sup id="cite_ref-Matthews85_220-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Matthews85-220">&#91;202&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-222" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-222">&#91;n 19&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Unlike his English predecessors such as <a href="/wiki/Edward_Elgar" title="Edward Elgar">Elgar</a> and Vaughan Williams, and composers from mainland Europe whom he admired, including Mahler and Shostakovich, Britten was not a classical symphonist. His youthful <i>jeux d\'esprit</i> the <i>Simple Symphony</i> (1934) is in conventional symphonic structure, observing <a href="/wiki/Sonata_form" title="Sonata form">sonata form</a> and the traditional four-movement pattern, but of his mature works his <i>Spring Symphony</i> (1949) is more a song cycle than a true symphony,<sup id="cite_ref-grove_61-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-grove-61">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> and the concertante <a href="/wiki/Cello_Symphony_(Britten)" title="Cello Symphony (Britten)">Cello Symphony</a> (1963) is an attempt to balance the traditional concerto and symphony. During its four movements the Cello Symphony moves from a deeply pessimistic opening to a finale of radiant happiness rare for Britten by this point.<sup id="cite_ref-223" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-223">&#91;204&#93;</a></sup> The composer considered it "the finest thing I\'ve written".<sup id="cite_ref-224" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-224">&#91;205&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Piano_Concerto_(Britten)" title="Piano Concerto (Britten)">Piano Concerto</a> (1938) was at first criticised for being too light-hearted and virtuoso. In 1945 Britten revised it, replacing a skittish third movement with a more sombre <a href="/wiki/Passacaglia" title="Passacaglia">passacaglia</a> that, in Matthews\'s view, gives the work more depth, and makes the apparent triumph of the finale more ambivalent.<sup id="cite_ref-225" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-225">&#91;206&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Violin_Concerto_(Britten)" title="Violin Concerto (Britten)">Violin Concerto</a> (1940) has virtuoso elements, but they are balanced by lyrical and elegiac passages, "undoubtedly reflecting Britten\'s growing concern with the escalation of world hostilities."<sup id="cite_ref-vc_226-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vc-226">&#91;207&#93;</a></sup> Neither concerto is among Britten\'s most popular works, but in the 21st century the Violin Concerto has been performed more frequently than before, both in the concert hall and on record.<sup id="cite_ref-vc_226-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vc-226">&#91;207&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Britten\'s incidental music for theatre, film and radio, much of it unpublished, was the subject of an essay by William Mann, published in 1952 in the first detailed critical assessment of Britten\'s music to that date.<sup id="cite_ref-227" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-227">&#91;208&#93;</a></sup> Of these pieces the music for a radio play, <i>The Rescue</i>, by <a href="/wiki/Edward_Sackville-West,_5th_Baron_Sackville" title="Edward Sackville-West, 5th Baron Sackville">Edward Sackville-West</a>, is praised by the musicologist Lewis Foreman as "of such stature and individual character as to be worth a regular place alongside [Britten\'s] other dramatic scores."<sup id="cite_ref-Foreman_228-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Foreman-228">&#91;209&#93;</a></sup> Mann finds in this score pre-echoes of the second act of <i>Billy Budd</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-229" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-229">&#91;210&#93;</a></sup> while Foreman observes that Britten "appears to have made passing allusions to <i>The Rescue</i> in his final opera, <i>Death in Venice</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Foreman_228-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Foreman-228">&#91;209&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chamber_and_instrumental_works">Chamber and instrumental works</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Benjamin_Britten&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Chamber and instrumental works">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Britten\'s close friendship with Rostropovich inspired the <a href="/wiki/Cello_Sonata_(Britten)" title="Cello Sonata (Britten)">Cello Sonata</a> (1961) and three suites for solo cello (1964\xe2\x80\x9371).<sup id="cite_ref-230" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-230">&#91;211&#93;</a></sup> String quartets featured throughout Britten\'s composing career, from a student work in 1928 to his <a href="/wiki/String_Quartet_No._3_(Britten)" title="String Quartet No. 3 (Britten)">Third String Quartet</a> (1975). The second Quartet, from 1945, was written in homage to Purcell; Mason considered it Britten\'s most important instrumental work to that date.<sup id="cite_ref-mason2_201-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mason2-201">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup> Referring to this work, Keller writes of the ease with which Britten, relatively early in his compositional career, solves "the modern sonata problem \xe2\x80\x93 the achievement of symmetry and unity within an extended ternary circle based on more than one subject". Keller likens the innovatory skill of the Quartet to that of <a href="/wiki/William_Walton" title="William Walton">Walton</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Viola_Concerto_(Walton)" title="Viola Concerto (Walton)">Viola Concerto</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-231" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-231">&#91;212&#93;</a></sup> The third Quartet was Britten\'s last major work; the critic Colin Anderson said of it in 2007, "one of Britten\'s greatest achievements, one with interesting allusions to <a href="/wiki/B%C3%A9la_Bart%C3%B3k" title="B\xc3\xa9la Bart\xc3\xb3k">Bart\xc3\xb3k</a> and Shostakovich, and written with an economy that opens out a depth of emotion that can be quite chilling."<sup id="cite_ref-232" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-232">&#91;213&#93;</a></sup> The <i>Gemini Variations</i> (1965), for flute, violin and piano duet, were based on a theme of <a href="/wiki/Zolt%C3%A1n_Kod%C3%A1ly" title="Zolt\xc3\xa1n Kod\xc3\xa1ly">Zolt\xc3\xa1n Kod\xc3\xa1ly</a> and written as a virtuoso piece for the 13-year-old Jeney twins, musical prodigies whom Britten had met in Budapest in the previous year.<sup id="cite_ref-233" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-233">&#91;214&#93;</a></sup> For <a href="/wiki/Osian_Ellis" title="Osian Ellis">Osian Ellis</a>, Britten wrote the Suite for Harp (1969), which <a href="/wiki/Joan_Chissell" title="Joan Chissell">Joan Chissell</a> in <i>The Times</i> described as "a little masterpiece of concentrated fancy".<sup id="cite_ref-234" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-234">&#91;215&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Benjamin_Britten&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Legacy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Snape_Maltings_Concert_Hall,_Snape,_Suffolk_(2).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Snape_Maltings_Concert_Hall%2C_Snape%2C_Suffolk_%282%29.jpg/220px-Snape_Maltings_Concert_Hall%2C_Snape%2C_Suffolk_%282%29.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Snape_Maltings_Concert_Hall%2C_Snape%2C_Suffolk_%282%29.jpg/330px-Snape_Maltings_Concert_Hall%2C_Snape%2C_Suffolk_%282%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Snape_Maltings_Concert_Hall%2C_Snape%2C_Suffolk_%282%29.jpg/440px-Snape_Maltings_Concert_Hall%2C_Snape%2C_Suffolk_%282%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2560" data-file-height="1920" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Snape_Maltings_Concert_Hall,_Snape,_Suffolk_(2).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Snape_Maltings" title="Snape Maltings">Snape Maltings</a> concert hall, a main venue of the <a href="/wiki/Aldeburgh_Festival" title="Aldeburgh Festival">Aldeburgh Festival</a>, founded by Britten, Pears and Crozier</div></div></div>\n<p>Britten\'s fellow-composers had divided views about him. To Tippett he was "simply the most musical person I have ever met", with an "incredible" technical mastery;<sup id="cite_ref-235" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-235">&#91;216&#93;</a></sup> some contemporaries, however, were less effusive. In Tippett\'s view Walton and others were convinced that Britten and Pears were leaders of a homosexual conspiracy in music,<sup id="cite_ref-237" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-237">&#91;n 20&#93;</a></sup> a belief Tippett dismisses as ridiculous, inspired by jealousy at Britten\'s postwar successes.<sup id="cite_ref-238" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-238">&#91;218&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Leonard_Bernstein" title="Leonard Bernstein">Leonard Bernstein</a> considered Britten "a man at odds with the world", and said of his music: "[I]f you hear it, not just listen to it superficially, you become aware of something very dark".<sup id="cite_ref-239" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-239">&#91;219&#93;</a></sup> The tenor <a href="/wiki/Robert_Tear" title="Robert Tear">Robert Tear</a>, who was closely associated with Britten in the latter part of the composer\'s career, made a similar point: "There was a great, huge abyss in his soul&#160;... He got into the valley of the shadow of death and couldn\'t get out".<sup id="cite_ref-Carpenter590_240-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Carpenter590-240">&#91;220&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the decade after Britten\'s death, his standing as a composer in Britain was to some extent overshadowed by that of the still-living Tippett.<sup id="cite_ref-241" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-241">&#91;221&#93;</a></sup> The film-maker <a href="/wiki/Tony_Palmer" title="Tony Palmer">Tony Palmer</a>   thought that Tippett\'s temporary ascendancy might have been a question of the two composers\' contrasting personalities: Tippett had more warmth and had made fewer enemies. In any event this was a short-lived phenomenon; Tippett adherents such as the composer <a href="/wiki/Robert_Saxton" title="Robert Saxton">Robert Saxton</a> soon rediscovered their enthusiasm for Britten, whose audience steadily increased during the final years of the 20th century.<sup id="cite_ref-Carpenter590_240-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Carpenter590-240">&#91;220&#93;</a></sup> Britten has had few imitators; Brett describes him as "inimitable, possessed of&#160;... a voice and sound too dangerous to imitate".<sup id="cite_ref-grove_61-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-grove-61">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> Nevertheless, after his death Britten was lauded by the younger generation of English composers to whom, in the words of <a href="/wiki/Oliver_Knussen" title="Oliver Knussen">Oliver Knussen</a>, he became "a phenomenal father-figure".<sup id="cite_ref-Carpenter590_240-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Carpenter590-240">&#91;220&#93;</a></sup> Brett believes that he affected every subsequent British composer to some extent: "He is a key figure in the growth of British musical culture in the second half of the 20th century, and his effect on everything from opera to the revitalization of music education is hard to overestimate."<sup id="cite_ref-grove_61-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-grove-61">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Whittall believes that one reason for Britten\'s enduring popularity is the "progressive conservatism" of his music. He generally avoided the avant garde, and did not challenge the conventions in the way that contemporaries such as Tippett did.<sup id="cite_ref-242" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-242">&#91;222&#93;</a></sup> Perhaps, says Brett, "the tide that swept away serialism, atonality and most forms of musical modernism and brought in neo-Romanticism, minimalism and other modes of expression involved with tonality carried with it renewed interest in composers who had been out of step with the times".<sup id="cite_ref-grove_61-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-grove-61">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> Britten defined his mission as a composer in very simple terms: composers should aim at "pleasing people today as seriously as we can".<sup id="cite_ref-243" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-243">&#91;223&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Pianist_and_conductor">Pianist and conductor</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Benjamin_Britten&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Pianist and conductor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Britten, though a reluctant conductor and a nervous pianist, was greatly sought after in both capacities.<sup id="cite_ref-244" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-244">&#91;224&#93;</a></sup> The piano accompanist <a href="/wiki/Gerald_Moore" title="Gerald Moore">Gerald Moore</a> wrote in his memoirs about playing at all the main music festivals except for Aldeburgh, because "as the presiding genius there is the greatest accompanist in the world, my services are not needed".<sup id="cite_ref-245" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-245">&#91;225&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-247" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-247">&#91;n 21&#93;</a></sup> Britten\'s recital partnership with Pears was his best-known collaboration, but he also accompanied <a href="/wiki/Kathleen_Ferrier" title="Kathleen Ferrier">Kathleen Ferrier</a>, Rostropovich, <a href="/wiki/Dietrich_Fischer-Dieskau" title="Dietrich Fischer-Dieskau">Dietrich Fischer-Dieskau</a>, <a href="/wiki/James_Bowman_(countertenor)" title="James Bowman (countertenor)">James Bowman</a> and <a href="/wiki/John_Shirley-Quirk" title="John Shirley-Quirk">John Shirley-Quirk</a>, among others.<sup id="cite_ref-decca_248-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-decca-248">&#91;227&#93;</a></sup> Though usually too nervous to play piano solos, Britten often performed piano duets with <a href="/wiki/Clifford_Curzon" title="Clifford Curzon">Clifford Curzon</a> or Richter, and chamber music with the <a href="/wiki/Amadeus_Quartet" title="Amadeus Quartet">Amadeus Quartet</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-decca_248-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-decca-248">&#91;227&#93;</a></sup> The composers whose works, other than his own, he most often played were Mozart and <a href="/wiki/Franz_Schubert" title="Franz Schubert">Schubert</a>; the latter, in <a href="/wiki/Murray_Perahia" title="Murray Perahia">Murray Perahia</a>\'s view, was Britten\'s greatest idol.<sup id="cite_ref-249" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-249">&#91;228&#93;</a></sup> As a boy and young man, Britten had intensely admired Brahms, but his admiration waned to nothing, and Brahms seldom featured in his repertory.<sup id="cite_ref-251" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-251">&#91;n 22&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Singers and players admired Britten\'s conducting, and <a href="/wiki/David_Webster_(opera_manager)" title="David Webster (opera manager)">David Webster</a> rated it highly enough to offer him the musical directorship of the <a href="/wiki/The_Royal_Opera" title="The Royal Opera">Covent Garden Opera</a> in 1952.<sup id="cite_ref-254" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-254">&#91;n 23&#93;</a></sup> Britten declined; he was not confident of his ability as a conductor and was reluctant to spend too much time performing rather than composing.<sup id="cite_ref-255" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-255">&#91;232&#93;</a></sup> As a conductor, Britten\'s repertory included Purcell, Bach, Haydn, Mozart and Schubert, and occasional less characteristic choices including <a href="/wiki/Robert_Schumann" title="Robert Schumann">Schumann</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Scenes_from_Goethe%27s_Faust" title="Scenes from Goethe&#39;s Faust">Scenes from Goethe\'s Faust</a></i>; Elgar\'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Dream_of_Gerontius" title="The Dream of Gerontius">The Dream of Gerontius</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Introduction_and_Allegro_(Elgar)" title="Introduction and Allegro (Elgar)">Introduction and Allegro</a></i>; <a href="/wiki/Gustav_Holst" title="Gustav Holst">Holst</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Egdon_Heath_(Holst)" title="Egdon Heath (Holst)">Egdon Heath</a></i> and short pieces by Percy Grainger.<sup id="cite_ref-decca_248-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-decca-248">&#91;227&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-256" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-256">&#91;233&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Recordings">Recordings</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Benjamin_Britten&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Recordings">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Britten, like Elgar and Walton before him, was signed up by a major British recording company,<sup id="cite_ref-259" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-259">&#91;n 24&#93;</a></sup> and performed a considerable proportion of his output on disc. For the <a href="/wiki/Decca_Records" title="Decca Records">Decca Record Company</a> he made some <a href="/wiki/Monaural" title="Monaural">monaural</a> records in the 1940s and 1950s, followed, with the enthusiastic support of the Decca producer <a href="/wiki/John_Culshaw" title="John Culshaw">John Culshaw</a>, by numerous <a href="/wiki/Stereophonic" class="mw-redirect" title="Stereophonic">stereophonic</a> versions of his works.<sup id="cite_ref-decca_248-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-decca-248">&#91;227&#93;</a></sup> Culshaw wrote, "The happiest hours I have spent in any studio were with Ben, for the basic reason that it did not seem that we were trying to make records or video tapes; we were just trying to make music."<sup id="cite_ref-260" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-260">&#91;236&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-262" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-262">&#91;n 25&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In May 1943 Britten made his debut in the Decca studios, accompanying <a href="/wiki/Sophie_Wyss" title="Sophie Wyss">Sophie Wyss</a> in five of his arrangements of French folk songs. The following January he and Pears recorded together, in Britten\'s arrangements of British folk songs, and the following day, in duet with Curzon he recorded his <i>Introduction and Rondo alla burlesca</i> and <i>Mazurka elegiaca</i>. In May 1944 he conducted the <a href="/wiki/Boyd_Neel" title="Boyd Neel">Boyd Neel</a> string orchestra, <a href="/wiki/Dennis_Brain" title="Dennis Brain">Dennis Brain</a> and Pears in the first recording of the <i>Serenade for Tenor, Horn and Strings</i>, which has frequently been reissued, most recently on CD.<sup id="cite_ref-decca_248-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-decca-248">&#91;227&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Britten\'s first operatic recording was <i>The Turn of the Screw</i>, made in January 1955 with the original English Opera Group forces. In 1957 he conducted <i>The Prince of the Pagodas</i> in an early stereo recording, supervised by Culshaw.<sup id="cite_ref-decca_248-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-decca-248">&#91;227&#93;</a></sup> Decca\'s first major commercial success with Britten came the following year, with <i>Peter Grimes</i>, which has, at 2013, never been out of the catalogues since its first release.<sup id="cite_ref-decca_248-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-decca-248">&#91;227&#93;</a></sup> From 1958 Britten conducted Decca recordings of many of his operas and vocal and orchestral works, including the <i>Nocturne</i> (1959), the <i>Spring Symphony</i> (1960) and the <i>War Requiem</i> (1963).<sup id="cite_ref-decca_248-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-decca-248">&#91;227&#93;</a></sup> The last sold in unexpectedly large numbers for a classical set, and thereafter Decca unstintingly made resources available to Culshaw and his successors for Britten recordings.<sup id="cite_ref-263" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-263">&#91;238&#93;</a></sup> Sets followed of <i>Albert Herring</i> (1964), the <i>Sinfonia da Requiem</i> (1964), <i>Curlew River</i> (1965), <i>A Midsummer Night\'s Dream</i> (1966), <i>The Burning Fiery Furnace</i> (1967), <i>Billy Budd</i> (1967) and many of the other major works.<sup id="cite_ref-decca_248-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-decca-248">&#91;227&#93;</a></sup> In 2013, to mark the anniversary of Britten\'s birth, Decca released a set of 65 CDs and one DVD, "Benjamin Britten \xe2\x80\x93 Complete Works".<sup id="cite_ref-265" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-265">&#91;n 26&#93;</a></sup> Most of the recordings were from Decca\'s back catalogue, but in the interests of comprehensiveness a substantial number of tracks were licensed from 20 other companies including <a href="/wiki/EMI_Classics" title="EMI Classics">EMI</a>, <a href="/wiki/Virgin_Classics" title="Virgin Classics">Virgin Classics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Naxos_Records" title="Naxos Records">Naxos</a>, <a href="/wiki/Warner_Music_Group" title="Warner Music Group">Warner</a> and <a href="/wiki/NMC_Recordings" title="NMC Recordings">NMC</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-complete_264-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-complete-264">&#91;239&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>As a pianist and conductor in other composers\' music, Britten made many recordings for Decca. Among his studio collaborations with Pears are sets of Schubert\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Winterreise" title="Winterreise">Winterreise</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Die_sch%C3%B6ne_M%C3%BCllerin" title="Die sch\xc3\xb6ne M\xc3\xbcllerin">Die sch\xc3\xb6ne M\xc3\xbcllerin</a></i>, Schumann\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Dichterliebe" title="Dichterliebe">Dichterliebe</a></i>, and songs by Haydn, Mozart, Bridge, Ireland, Holst, <a href="/wiki/Michael_Tippett" title="Michael Tippett">Tippett</a> and <a href="/wiki/Richard_Rodney_Bennett" title="Richard Rodney Bennett">Richard Rodney Bennett</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-decca_248-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-decca-248">&#91;227&#93;</a></sup> Other soloists whom Britten accompanied on record were Ferrier, Rostropovich and Vishnevskaya. As a conductor he recorded a wide range of composers, from Purcell to Grainger. Among his best-known Decca recordings are Purcell\'s <i>The Fairy-Queen</i>, Bach\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Brandenburg_concertos" class="mw-redirect" title="Brandenburg concertos">Brandenburg concertos</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/S%C3%BC%C3%9Fer_Trost,_mein_Jesus_k%C3%B6mmt,_BWV_151" title="S\xc3\xbc\xc3\x9fer Trost, mein Jesus k\xc3\xb6mmt, BWV 151">Cantata 151</a>, <a href="/wiki/Herr,_deine_Augen_sehen_nach_dem_Glauben,_BWV_102" title="Herr, deine Augen sehen nach dem Glauben, BWV 102">Cantata 102</a> and <i><a href="/wiki/St_John_Passion" title="St John Passion">St John Passion</a></i>, Elgar\'s <i>The Dream of Gerontius</i> and Mozart\'s last two symphonies.<sup id="cite_ref-decca_248-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-decca-248">&#91;227&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span id="Honours.2C_awards_and_commemorations"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Honours,_awards_and_commemorations">Honours, awards and commemorations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Benjamin_Britten&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Honours, awards and commemorations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Benjamin_Britten_137_Cromwell_Road_blue_plaque.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Benjamin_Britten_137_Cromwell_Road_blue_plaque.jpg/220px-Benjamin_Britten_137_Cromwell_Road_blue_plaque.jpg" width="220" height="208" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Benjamin_Britten_137_Cromwell_Road_blue_plaque.jpg/330px-Benjamin_Britten_137_Cromwell_Road_blue_plaque.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Benjamin_Britten_137_Cromwell_Road_blue_plaque.jpg/440px-Benjamin_Britten_137_Cromwell_Road_blue_plaque.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2343" data-file-height="2215" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Benjamin_Britten_137_Cromwell_Road_blue_plaque.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>137 <a href="/wiki/Cromwell_Road" title="Cromwell Road">Cromwell Road</a> blue plaque</div></div></div>\n<p>State honours awarded to Britten included <a href="/wiki/Order_of_the_Companions_of_Honour" title="Order of the Companions of Honour">Companion of Honour</a> (Britain) in 1953;<sup id="cite_ref-266" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-266">&#91;240&#93;</a></sup> Commander of the <a href="/wiki/Order_of_the_Polar_Star" title="Order of the Polar Star">Royal Order of the Polar Star</a> (Sweden) in 1962; the <a href="/wiki/Order_of_Merit" title="Order of Merit">Order of Merit</a> (Britain) in 1965;<sup id="cite_ref-267" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-267">&#91;241&#93;</a></sup> and a <a href="/wiki/Life_peer" title="Life peer">life peerage</a>  (Britain) in July 1976.<sup id="cite_ref-TheLondon_132-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TheLondon-132">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> He received honorary degrees and fellowships from 19 conservatories and universities in Europe and America. His awards included the <a href="/wiki/Hanseatic_Goethe_Prize" title="Hanseatic Goethe Prize">Hanseatic Goethe Prize</a> (1961); the Aspen Award, Colorado (1964); the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Philharmonic_Society" title="Royal Philharmonic Society">Royal Philharmonic Society</a>\'s Gold Medal (1964); the <a href="/wiki/Wihuri_Sibelius_Prize" title="Wihuri Sibelius Prize">Wihuri Sibelius Prize</a> (1965); the Mahler Medal (Bruckner and Mahler Society of America, 1967); the <a href="/wiki/L%C3%A9onie_Sonning_Music_Prize" title="L\xc3\xa9onie Sonning Music Prize">L\xc3\xa9onie Sonning Music Prize</a> (Denmark, 1968); the <a href="/wiki/Ernst_von_Siemens_Music_Prize" title="Ernst von Siemens Music Prize">Ernst von Siemens Music Prize</a> (1974); and the Ravel Prize (1974).<sup id="cite_ref-268" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-268">&#91;242&#93;</a></sup> Prizes for individual works included <a href="/wiki/UNESCO" title="UNESCO">UNESCO</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/International_Rostrum_of_Composers" title="International Rostrum of Composers">International Rostrum of Composers</a> 1961 for <i>A Midsummer Night\'s Dream</i>; and <a href="/wiki/Grammy_Awards" class="mw-redirect" title="Grammy Awards">Grammy Awards</a> in 1963 and 1977 for the <i>War Requiem</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-269" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-269">&#91;243&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/The_Red_House,_Aldeburgh" title="The Red House, Aldeburgh">The Red House, Aldeburgh</a>, where Britten and Pears lived and worked together from 1957 until Britten\'s death in 1976, is now the home of the Britten-Pears Foundation, established to promote their musical legacy.<sup id="cite_ref-270" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-270">&#91;244&#93;</a></sup> In Britten\'s centenary year his studio at the Red House was restored to the way it was in the 1950s and opened to the public. The converted hayloft was designed and built by <a href="/wiki/H._T._Cadbury-Brown" title="H. T. Cadbury-Brown">H T Cadbury Brown</a> in 1958 and was described by Britten as a "magnificent work".<sup id="cite_ref-271" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-271">&#91;245&#93;</a></sup> In June 2013 Dame Janet Baker officially opened the Britten-Pears archive in a new building in the grounds of the Red House.<sup id="cite_ref-272" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-272">&#91;246&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Britten_Music_Academy" class="mw-redirect" title="Benjamin Britten Music Academy">Benjamin Britten Music Academy</a> in Lowestoft, founded in the composer\'s honour, was completed in 1979; it is an 11-18 co-educational day school, with ties to the Britten-Pears Foundation.<sup id="cite_ref-273" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-273">&#91;247&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Scallop,_Maggi_Hambling,_Aldeburgh.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/The_Scallop%2C_Maggi_Hambling%2C_Aldeburgh.jpg/220px-The_Scallop%2C_Maggi_Hambling%2C_Aldeburgh.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/The_Scallop%2C_Maggi_Hambling%2C_Aldeburgh.jpg/330px-The_Scallop%2C_Maggi_Hambling%2C_Aldeburgh.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/The_Scallop%2C_Maggi_Hambling%2C_Aldeburgh.jpg/440px-The_Scallop%2C_Maggi_Hambling%2C_Aldeburgh.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1156" data-file-height="768" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Scallop,_Maggi_Hambling,_Aldeburgh.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Scallop</i> by <a href="/wiki/Maggi_Hambling" title="Maggi Hambling">Maggi Hambling</a> is a sculpture dedicated to Benjamin Britten on the beach at <a href="/wiki/Aldeburgh" title="Aldeburgh">Aldeburgh</a>. The edge of the shell is pierced with the words "I hear those voices that will not be drowned" from <i>Peter Grimes</i>.</div></div></div>\n<p>A memorial stone to Britten was unveiled in the north choir aisle of Westminster Abbey in 1978.<sup id="cite_ref-274" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-274">&#91;248&#93;</a></sup> There are memorial plaques to him at three of his London homes: 173 Cromwell Road,<sup id="cite_ref-275" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-275">&#91;249&#93;</a></sup> 45a <a href="/wiki/St_John%27s_Wood" title="St John&#39;s Wood">St John\'s Wood</a> High Street,<sup id="cite_ref-276" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-276">&#91;250&#93;</a></sup> and 8 Halliford Street in <a href="/wiki/Islington" title="Islington">Islington</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-277" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-277">&#91;251&#93;</a></sup> In April 2013 Britten was honoured by the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Mail" title="Royal Mail">Royal Mail</a> in the UK, as one of ten people selected as subjects for the "Great Britons" <a href="/wiki/Great_Britain_commemorative_stamps_2010%E2%80%932019" class="mw-redirect" title="Great Britain commemorative stamps 2010\xe2\x80\x932019">commemorative postage stamp</a> issue.<sup id="cite_ref-278" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-278">&#91;252&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Other creative artists have celebrated Britten. In 1970 Walton composed <i>Improvisations on an Impromptu of Benjamin Britten</i>, based on a theme from Britten\'s Piano Concerto.<sup id="cite_ref-279" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-279">&#91;253&#93;</a></sup> Works commemorating Britten include <i><a href="/wiki/Cantus_in_Memoriam_Benjamin_Britten" title="Cantus in Memoriam Benjamin Britten">Cantus in Memoriam Benjamin Britten</a></i> an orchestral piece written in 1977 by <a href="/wiki/Arvo_P%C3%A4rt" title="Arvo P\xc3\xa4rt">Arvo P\xc3\xa4rt</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sally_Beamish" title="Sally Beamish">Sally Beamish</a>\'s <i>Variations on a Theme of Benjamin Britten</i>, based on the second Sea Interlude from <i>Peter Grimes</i>; she composed the work to mark Britten\'s centenary.<sup id="cite_ref-280" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-280">&#91;254&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Alan_Bennett" title="Alan Bennett">Alan Bennett</a> depicts Britten in a 2009 play <i><a href="/wiki/The_Habit_of_Art" title="The Habit of Art">The Habit of Art</a></i>, set while Britten is composing <i>Death in Venice</i> and centred on a fictional meeting between Britten and Auden. Britten was played in the premiere production by <a href="/wiki/Alex_Jennings" title="Alex Jennings">Alex Jennings</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-281" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-281">&#91;255&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Tony Palmer made three documentary films about Britten: <i>Benjamin Britten &amp; his Festival</i> (1967);<sup id="cite_ref-282" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-282">&#91;256&#93;</a></sup> <i>A Time There Was</i> (1979);<sup id="cite_ref-283" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-283">&#91;257&#93;</a></sup> and <i>Nocturne</i> (2013).<sup id="cite_ref-284" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-284">&#91;258&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Centenary">Centenary</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Benjamin_Britten&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Centenary">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In September 2012, to mark the composer\'s forthcoming centenary, the Britten-Pears Foundation launched "Britten 100", a collaboration of leading organisations in the performing arts, publishing, broadcasting, film, academia and heritage.<sup id="cite_ref-285" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-285">&#91;259&#93;</a></sup> Among the events were the release of a feature film <i>Benjamin Britten \xe2\x80\x93 Peace and Conflict</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-286" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-286">&#91;260&#93;</a></sup> and a centenary exhibition at the <a href="/wiki/British_Library" title="British Library">British Library</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-287" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-287">&#91;261&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Royal_Mint" title="Royal Mint">Royal Mint</a> issued a <a href="/wiki/Fifty_pence_(British_coin)" title="Fifty pence (British coin)">50-pence</a> piece, to mark the centenary \xe2\x80\x93 the first time a composer has featured on a British coin.<sup id="cite_ref-288" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-288">&#91;262&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Centenary performances of the <i>War Requiem</i> were given at eighteen locations in Britain. Opera productions included <i>Owen Wingrave</i> at Aldeburgh, <i>Billy Budd</i> at Glyndebourne, <i>Death in Venice</i> by <a href="/wiki/English_National_Opera" title="English National Opera">English National Opera</a>, <i>Gloriana</i> by <a href="/wiki/The_Royal_Opera" title="The Royal Opera">The Royal Opera</a>, and <i>Peter Grimes</i>, <i>Death in Venice</i> and <i>A Midsummer Night\'s Dream</i> by <a href="/wiki/Opera_North" title="Opera North">Opera North</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-events_289-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-events-289">&#91;263&#93;</a></sup> <i>Peter Grimes</i> was performed on the beach at Aldeburgh, opening the 2013 Aldeburgh Festival in June 2013, with <a href="/wiki/Steuart_Bedford" title="Steuart Bedford">Steuart Bedford</a> conducting and singers from the Chorus of <a href="/wiki/Opera_North" title="Opera North">Opera North</a> and the Chorus of the <a href="/wiki/Guildhall_School_of_Music_and_Drama" title="Guildhall School of Music and Drama">Guildhall School of Music and Drama</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-290" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-290">&#91;264&#93;</a></sup> described by <i><a href="/wiki/The_Guardian" title="The Guardian">The Guardian</a></i> as "a remarkable, and surely unrepeatable achievement."<sup id="cite_ref-291" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-291">&#91;265&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Internationally, the anniversary was marked by performances of the <i>War Requiem</i>, <i>Peter Grimes</i> and other works in four continents. In the US the centennial events were described as "coast to coast," with a Britten festival at <a href="/wiki/Carnegie_Hall" title="Carnegie Hall">Carnegie Hall</a>, and performances at the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Philharmonic" title="New York Philharmonic">New York Philharmonic</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Metropolitan_Opera" title="Metropolitan Opera">Metropolitan Opera</a> and <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles_Opera" title="Los Angeles Opera">Los Angeles Opera</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-292" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-292">&#91;266&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span id="Notes.2C_references_and_sources"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes,_references_and_sources">Notes, references and sources</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Benjamin_Britten&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Notes, references and sources">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p><b>Notes</b>\n</p>\n<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 25em; -webkit-column-width: 25em; column-width: 25em; list-style-type: decimal;">\n<ol class="references">\n<li id="cite_note-4"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-4">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Britten\'s siblings were (Edith) Barbara (1902\xe2\x80\x9382), Robert Harry Marsh ("Bobby", 1907\xe2\x80\x9387), and (Charlotte) Elizabeth ("Beth", 1909\xe2\x80\x9389).<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-28"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-28">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Britten later gave an example of the detailed skill instilled in him by Bridge: "I came up with a series of <a href="/wiki/Major_seventh" title="Major seventh">major sevenths</a> on the violin. Bridge was against this, saying that the instrument didn\'t vibrate properly with this interval: it should be divided between two instruments".<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-34"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-34">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">When it came to leaving Gresham\'s, Britten found it a wrench, confessing: "I am terribly sorry to leave such boys as these. [...] I didn\'t think I should be so sorry to leave."<sup id="cite_ref-bridcut17_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bridcut17-33">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> In his later years, Britten helped secure a place at the school for <a href="/wiki/David_Hemmings" title="David Hemmings">David Hemmings</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-bridcut17_33-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bridcut17-33">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-39"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-39">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">This academic mistrust of Britten\'s technical skills persisted. In 1994 the critic Derrick Puffett wrote that in the 1960s Britten was still regarded with suspicion on account of his technical expertise; Puffett quoted remarks by the Professor of Music at Oxford in the 1960s, <a href="/wiki/Jack_Westrup" title="Jack Westrup">Sir Jack Westrup</a>, to the effect that Britten was to be distrusted for his "superficial effects", whereas Tippett was considered "awkward and technically unskilled but somehow authentic."<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-43"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-43">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Britten later wrote about his youthful discovery of Mahler that he had been told that the composer was "long-winded and formless&#160;... a romantic self-indulgent, who was so infatuated with his ideas that he could never stop. Either he couldn\'t score at all, or he could only score like Wagner, using enormous orchestras with so much going on that you couldn\'t hear anything clearly. Above all, he was not original. In other words, nothing for a young student!" Britten judged, on the contrary, "His influence on contemporary writing&#160;... could only be beneficial.
