His style is free from excessive personal mannerisms, and his scores are models of how the modern virtuoso orchestra should be used, nothing being left to chance and every note sounding."<sup id="cite_ref-mahler_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mahler-42">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-73"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-73">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Koussevitzky\'s generosity later extended to waiving his rights to mount the first production, allowing Britten and his <a href="/wiki/English_National_Opera" title="English National Opera">Sadler\'s Wells</a> associates the chance to do so. The opera\'s first performance under Koussevitzky\'s aegis was at the <a href="/wiki/Tanglewood_Music_Festival" title="Tanglewood Music Festival">Tanglewood Music Festival</a> in 1947, conducted by the young <a href="/wiki/Leonard_Bernstein" title="Leonard Bernstein">Leonard Bernstein</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> Bernstein retained a love of the work, and he conducted the orchestral "Sea Interludes" from the opera at his final concert, given in Tanglewood in 1990, shortly before his death.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-77"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-77">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Sadler%27s_Wells_Theatre" title="Sadler&#39;s Wells Theatre">Sadler\'s Wells Theatre</a> in <a href="/wiki/Islington" title="Islington">Islington</a>, London, was requisitioned by the government in 1942 as a refuge for people made homeless by air-raids; the Sadler\'s Wells opera company toured the British provinces, returning to its home base in June 1945.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-99"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-99">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Arthur_Sullivan" title="Arthur Sullivan">Sullivan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hubert_Parry" title="Hubert Parry">Parry</a>, <a href="/wiki/Charles_Villiers_Stanford" title="Charles Villiers Stanford">Stanford</a>, <a href="/wiki/Edward_Elgar" title="Edward Elgar">Elgar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ralph_Vaughan_Williams" title="Ralph Vaughan Williams">Vaughan Williams</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gustav_Holst" title="Gustav Holst">Holst</a> and <a href="/wiki/Michael_Tippett" title="Michael Tippett">Tippett</a> were among the leading British composers of their time who held posts at conservatoires or universities.<sup id="cite_ref-wright_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wright-96">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> Those who, like Britten, were not known for teaching included <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Delius" title="Frederick Delius">Delius</a><sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/William_Walton" title="William Walton">Walton</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-105"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-105">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The critic <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Porter_(music_critic)" title="Andrew Porter (music critic)">Andrew Porter</a> wrote at the time: "The audience naturally contained many people distinguished in political and social spheres rather than noted for their appreciation of twentieth-century music, and <i>Gloriana</i> was not well received at its first hearing. The usual philistine charges brought against it, as against so much contemporary music (\'no tunes \xe2\x80\x93 ugly, discordant sounds\', and the rest), are beneath consideration. On the other hand, those who found <i>Gloriana</i> ill-suited to the occasion may be allowed to have some right on their side."<sup id="cite_ref-hso_103-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hso-103">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-112"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-112">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The principal law against homosexual acts was the <a href="/wiki/Criminal_Law_Amendment_Act_1885" title="Criminal Law Amendment Act 1885">Criminal Law Amendment Act 1885</a>, in which <a href="/wiki/Labouchere_Amendment" title="Labouchere Amendment">Section 11</a> made any kind of sexual activity between men illegal for the first time. It was not repealed until the passage of the <a href="/wiki/Sexual_Offences_Act_1967" title="Sexual Offences Act 1967">Sexual Offences Act 1967</a></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-135"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-135">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Some writers have supposed that Britten was earlier offered and had declined a <a href="/wiki/Knighthood" class="mw-redirect" title="Knighthood">knighthood</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> but his name is not included in the official list issued in 2012 by the <a href="/wiki/Cabinet_Office" title="Cabinet Office">Cabinet Office</a> naming everyone (except those still living at the time of publication) who had declined an honour between 1950 and 1999.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-156"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-156">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The writer John Bridcut sees significance in evidence that Britten mentally regarded himself as perpetually 13 years old. Bridcut views this as manifest both in the Letts diaries Britten bought and used well into his adult life, in which he wrote several statistics relevant to himself when that age,<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup> and in his remark to Imogen Holst, "I\'m still thirteen".<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-160"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-160">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In the early 1940s, while living in North America, Britten shared a room with 13 year old Bobby Rothman when staying with the Rothman family: "many an evening we used to spend&#160;... a lot of time just really talking he in the bed next to me&#160;... His fondness for me was something that was beyond my normal social connections, and I was a little overwhelmed that someone should be so fond of me&#160;... I can still remember us talking late at night one time, and finding when it was really time to call it quits and go to sleep&#160;... he said, \'Bobby, would you mind terribly if, before we fell asleep, I came over and gave you a hug and a kiss?\' It was just one of those touching moments&#160;... And I\'ve got to say I really did not know what to do except say, \'no, no I don\'t mind\', and he gently got up and gave me a gentle hug and kiss and said goodnight."<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-164"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-164">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The journalist Martin Kettle wrote in 2012 that although there is no evidence of wrongful conduct, it is important that allegations of <a href="/wiki/Paedophilia" class="mw-redirect" title="Paedophilia">paedophilia</a> should be openly discussed, both to avoid covering up criminal behaviour and to avoid oversimplifying the complexity of Britten\'s sexuality and creativity.<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-183"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-183">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In 1938, Britten attended what was only the second British performance of <a href="/wiki/Symphony_No._8_(Mahler)" title="Symphony No. 8 (Mahler)">Mahler\'s Eighth Symphony</a>, the "Symphony of a Thousand", with <a href="/wiki/Henry_Wood" title="Henry Wood">Sir Henry Wood</a> and the <a href="/wiki/BBC_Symphony_Orchestra" title="BBC Symphony Orchestra">BBC Symphony Orchestra</a>. Britten declared himself "tremendously impressed" by the music, though he thought the performance "execrable".<sup id="cite_ref-182" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-182">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-189"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-189">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The critics\' outrage at the presumption of Auden and Britten in writing an American work mirrored the hostile response of London critics six years earlier when <a href="/wiki/Jerome_Kern" title="Jerome Kern">Jerome Kern</a> and <a href="/wiki/Oscar_Hammerstein_II" title="Oscar Hammerstein II">Oscar Hammerstein</a> presented <i><a href="/wiki/Three_Sisters_(musical)" title="Three Sisters (musical)">Three Sisters</a></i>, a musical set in England.<sup id="cite_ref-188" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-188">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-200"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-200">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Matthews comments that the work is "so much more sensuous when sung by the soprano voice for which the songs were conceived".<sup id="cite_ref-M56_199-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-M56-199">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-210"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-210">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The piece was much admired by Tippett as "one of the wonderful things in Britten\'s music", an opinion with which Britten apparently concurred.<sup id="cite_ref-209" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-209">&#91;192&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-222"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-222">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The piece is formally sub-titled "Variations and Fugue on a Theme of Henry Purcell"; Britten greatly disliked the BBC\'s practice of referring to the work by the grander sub-title in preference to his preferred title.<sup id="cite_ref-221" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-221">&#91;203&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-237"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-237">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Steuart_Wilson" title="Steuart Wilson">Steuart Wilson</a>, a retired singer who held a succession of posts as a musical administrator, launched an outspoken campaign in 1955 against "homosexuality in British music" and was quoted as saying: "The influence of perverts in the world of music has grown beyond all measure. If it is not curbed soon, Covent Garden and other precious musical heritages could suffer irreparable harm."<sup id="cite_ref-236" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-236">&#91;217&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-247"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-247">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In 2006 <i><a href="/wiki/Gramophone_(magazine)" title="Gramophone (magazine)">Gramophone</a></i> magazine invited eminent present-day accompanists to name their "professional\'s professional": the joint winners were Britten and Moore.<sup id="cite_ref-246" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-246">&#91;226&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-251"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-251">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Britten once said, "It\'s not bad Brahms I mind, it\'s good Brahms I can\'t stand".<sup id="cite_ref-250" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-250">&#91;229&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-254"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-254">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">So writes John Bridcut,<sup id="cite_ref-252" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-252">&#91;230&#93;</a></sup> but Webster\'s biographer, <a href="/wiki/Montague_Haltrecht" title="Montague Haltrecht">Montague Haltrecht</a>, recounts that no formal offer of the post was made to Britten. According to Haltrecht, Lord Harewood and other Covent Garden board members wanted Britten for the post, but Webster believed that it was above all as a composer that Britten could bring glory to Covent Garden.<sup id="cite_ref-253" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-253">&#91;231&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-259"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-259">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Elgar was an exclusive <a href="/wiki/Gramophone_Company" title="Gramophone Company">HMV</a> artist;<sup id="cite_ref-257" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-257">&#91;234&#93;</a></sup> Walton, after a brief spell with Decca, made most of his recordings for <a href="/wiki/Columbia_Graphophone_Company" title="Columbia Graphophone Company">Columbia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-258" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-258">&#91;235&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-262"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-262">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Imogen Holst remembered Britten\'s recording sessions differently: "He used to find recording sessions more exhausting than anything else, and dreaded the days when he had to stop writing a new opera in order to record the one before last."<sup id="cite_ref-261" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-261">&#91;237&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-265"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-265">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The set comprises all the composer\'s works with opus numbers and all works commercially recorded by 2013 (many fragments and juvenilia have not been published or recorded). The set includes Britten\'s folksong arrangements, but excludes his Purcell realisations.<sup id="cite_ref-complete_264-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-complete-264">&#91;239&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n</ol></div>\n<p><b>References</b>\n</p>\n<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em; list-style-type: decimal;">\n<ol class="references">\n<li id="cite_note-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-1">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Matthews, p. 1</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-2"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-2">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Kennedy, p. 2</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-3"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-3">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Evans (2009), p. 513</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-5"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-5">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Powell, p. 3</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-6"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-6">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Carpenter, pp. 4 and 7;
